B005OCHOZS|5.0|Even if you don't care about ultrarunning or know anything about the sport, Eat & Run can be an entertaining and thought-provoking read.  It's more than just a story about a man who has been one of the most dominant ultrarunners in the world for the last decade, it's about a man who seeks to transform himself through dedication to his sport, a conscientious way of living (especially through diet) and searching always for greater insight into himself.<br /><br />Jurek began making a name for himself in the ultrarunning scene in the late 90s, when he first began winning big ultrarunning events.  He was featured in Christopher McDougall's bestseller, Born to Run, as he was one of the gringo runners who went up against the now-famous Tarahumara Indian runners in their native land.<br /><br />The book is entertaining as it details all the tribulations individuals go through who engage in extreme sports, who push themselves beyond limits most of us can't cross -- and do it time and again as a way of life.  The book, though, is also compelling as biography, and Jurek comes across as an unpretentious guy who comes from simple, working-class roots who had to deal with many challenges early in life (an overbearing father, relative poverty, a mother who was sick from MS) and because of those challenges, became a fierce competitor with a drive that is admirable to say the least.<br /><br />The format is a bit quirky, with chapters about running mixed with recollections about youthful experiences and then random vegan recipes sprinkled throughout.  There's also a lot of reflection/introspection, as Jurek melds existentialism with the runner's and vegan's way of life (without being preachy).  The fact that Jurek is vegan is initially amazing for what he puts his body through, but it makes sense once you understand that Jurek's whole approach to living is about simplicity and purity.<br /><br />The book was a great read and inspired me to re-think some of my own limitations.  I think it could do the same for you.

B005LAIGPA|4.0|Chronicle has your average young superhero movie origin formula, however they play it off with much better effect. The story revolves around three highschool kids that find this meteor or something underground and it bestows them with telekenesis. Two of the kids want to keep it as a little hobby they do with each other while one of them, after going through years of abuse, wants to use his powers for his own purposes.<br /><br />Chronicle's age old story was told really well, where the villian Andrew here is really fleshed out well as a sympathetic character, making his downfall that more tragic. RedLetterMedia pointed out that this whole story is essentially the same as the Star Wars prequels and yet it tells the story with much more conviction and excitment in less than 90 minutes, suck on that Lucas indeed. Alex (I think?) is Andrew's more popular cousin who tries to help Andrew out with his social situation, and Steve is the charismatic school president who happens to be with them when they discover the rock, all of them seemed stereotypical but it was the little flourishes here and there that made their characters more convicing than they should've been.<br /><br />The only real complaint I have though about Chronicle (and everyone else has covered it there and back again) is the found-footage style, it didn't really seem necessary to tell the story and it wasn't consistent. The first scene Andrew explains to his drunk dad that he bought a camera and is recording his dad being locked in his room, so what's he going to do with the camera, send it to the police? No, he's going to carry it around with him everywhere, recording everything. Why? Is he so lonely that he films himself? I've never heard of people doing that before. Also Alex's love interest happened to be a blog personality online that also happens to film everything, very coincidential for a movie that plays itself out for realism. Then near the end there's a few camera shots like when Alex opens his eyes after laying down with the police around him, there was no camera filming that, so they didn't even keep the 'found footage' style consistent.<br /><br />What they should've done instead was pick and choose what to film 'found footage' like and what not to, like when Andrew pushes the car into the river there's no reason he would've grabbed the camera in real life, so scenes like that just film them naturally, it's not a big deal if it's not all completely found footage. Some parts were interestingly shot too, like the final 'confrontation' was all shot through onlooker's cell phones or cameras, it was definately innovative and looked realistic, but I wanted a little more. So again, cut to some people's camera persepctive then cut back to the action, well I don't know, it was still damn good and I'm rambling again.<br /><br />Anyways Chronicle was entertaining the entire way through which is a gem of it's own, not one second I would say was filler. All the 'hataz' that say it was dumb teenage angst crap it's not, teen agnst is kids with first world problems brooding about getting grounded or something, Andrew had real problems and issues. And for the people saying it was slow: seriously? Anyways for normal people tired of seeing the same Superhero origin crap being vomited out into movie theaters everywhere Chronicle takes that concept and puts a new spin on it making it interesting again, definately worth a look.eyes after laying down with the police around him, there was no camera filming that, so they didn't even keep the 'found footage' style consistent.<br /><br />What they should've done instead was pick and choose what to film 'found footage' like and what not to, like when Andrew pushes the car into the river there's no reason he would've grabbed the camera in real life, so scenes like that just film them naturally, it's not a big deal if it's not all completely found footage. B0000B1OGD|5.0|Anyone who is fond of Ray Harryhausen stop-motion animation (also called \\"Dynamation\\") will obviously enjoy this flick for the very fine but typical  special effects.<br /><br />Although this is a \\"Cowboys Meet the Dinosaurs\\" flick - which is a novel idea, it follows pretty much the plots of earlier \\"captured giant monster\\" movies, most notably \\"King Kong\\" and \\"20 Million Miles to Earth\\".<br /><br />However, this one is a bit more entertaining because there is a backstory about a struggling Wild West show. Perhaps it is struggling because it's taking place IN the Wild West, or almost, if Mexico counts. They'd probably draw a better crowd back east in New York!<br /><br />It is through accident that the cowboys running the show discover the dinos in the \\"Forbidden Valley\\". (Didn't that become the name of a salad dressing?) There, they meet Gwangi (the star of the movie) whom they hope will save the show - or eat it - whichever comes first.<br /><br />It is in this backstory that we get to meet some interesting and charming characters, and this is what makes the movie for me.<br /><br />We have good main characters - Tuck (played by James Franciscus who does a very nice job as hero), T.J. (played by Gila Golan) the show's owner, main attraction and Tuck's love interest, Champ (played by an aging Richard Carlson who doesn't really have that much to do), Professor Bromley (played by Laurence Naismith with a British accent) and Lope, the enterprising Mexican boy, played by Curtis Arden.<br /><br />Since the dinos are very predictable in their look and behavior, it is the backstory and acting that carries the film.<br /><br />Franciscus is cast as a kind of lover boy/hunk. Yes, they get his shirt off in one scene and we get to see Gila Golan change clothes behind a screen in another. We also get to see them kiss, but only after explaining they want to marry, have kids, and start a ranch. This is a G-rated film, after all!<br /><br />Franciscus carries off the role pretty well, but where he shines is in his scenes with Lope, the Mexican boy. He never talks down to the kid or treats him as an \\"extra\\" (as often happens in movies of this type that have a kid - See \\"7th Voyage of Sinbad\\") and shows genuine concern for the kid's safety. He also is believable as a semi-rogue in his affections for TJ, his love interest. Theirs is an on-again, off-again relationship, with both succumbing to the allure of fame and fortune over their mutual affection. He plays well with the Professor, acting the foil while the Professor explains the more complex concepts of paleontology so we as audience can believe in Wild West Dinos.<br /><br />TJ really could be more developed (as a character, not physically, she is very well developed along those lines). She lacks the toughness of a hard headed business woman and show owner. But, her vulnerability (and naivete) make her a good foil for Tuck. If she had been a stronger character, their relationship would have carried more interest.<br /><br />The Professor is convincing as an opportunistic scientist, but he's never an evil scientist, just one whose scruples can be \\"adjusted\\" to meet his goals. The Professor also provides a bit of comic relief. What is missing from his character is that he does not act as the voice of science, reason or compassion for the dinos and their treatment. The script isn't that advanced (we don't see this type of intelligent behavior until Jeff Goldblum and Sam Neil show up in \\"Jurassic Park\\"). Nevertheless, he is a fun character to watch.<br /><br />Sadly, Champ (Richard Carlson, the veteran of sci-fi flicks), has little to do in the movie other than act as a father figure to TJ, although at times it seems he may have a love interest in her as well, even if it is only platonic.<br /><br />They say if you want a hit, stick in a kid or a puppy, or better yet both. We don't get a puppy here (does a cute little tiny horse count?), but Curtis Arden as Lope, the enterprising Mexican boydoes deliver the charm.<br /><br />Of the animated characters, Gwangi is by far the most alluring, if voracious. For some reason, most Hollywood dinos and similar giant monsters seem to have a different way of behaving than we would expect in the natural world. Gwangi is no different.<br /><br />We are supposed to believe he is an Allosaurus, a prehistoric predator. Obviously, Gwangi is hungry and in search of food. However, when he grabs and kills a smaller dinosaur (poor thing - the screaming is not pleasant to hear), he only takes a few bites and is then off to chase the smaller but far more interesting cowboys (it's the equivalent of taking a bite of a steak dinner, then chasing down a bowl of popcorn). Realistically, one might think the creature would ignore them and drag his meal off to eat it completely. But then it would be HOURS before we get a dino-chases-cowboys scene!<br /><br />While this movie is pale by comparison in violence to modern dino flicks (of the Jurassic Park type), it is none the less disturbing at times. This is thanks to the sound effects more than the action.<br /><br />There is only a minimum amount of blood and carnage shown, which one would expect from a movie of the late 1960's. However, there is one scene that is supposed to be a shocker, but comes across as almost amusing in spite of its violence.<br /><br />Gwangi is revealed to the public, chewing on a dwarf! Although this is meant to be shocking, and it certainly is memorable, it's so expected and over-the-top that it just made me laugh. The amusement isn't helped by Champ's (Richard Carlson) look of shocked dismay and his command to his boys, \\"Hurry, get the curtain down!\\"<br /><br />The real violence in this movie is aural. There is plenty of agonized screaming from creatures and humans alike in the killing scenes. It shows what a good sound crew can add to a film without falling back on gore and red dye glop gross out effects or ominous music blasts from the orchestra.<br /><br />Of course, in the end of the movie, Gwangi must be eliminated since the scientist has failed to argue a reasonable treatment of this remarkable and unique creature. We can't have Gwangi running around loose for obvious reasons, so he must die in a convenient, if accidental fire.<br /><br />Here the film falls back on the older favorites, \\"King Kong\\" and \\"20 Million Miles to Earth\\", but with Gwangi screaming and suffering a horrible death in the burning building.<br /><br />There is a sense of pity for the poor dino, who was only minding his own business eating other dinos before the meddlesome cowboys captured him. There is a blatant sense of unfairness here, made more poignant by the look on the actor's faces (Franciscus, Golen, Arden) as they hear the sounds of the dying creature.<br /><br />The films ends here leaving some questions.<br /><br />Who is going to pay for all the damage to the Town?<br />Will the scientist launch a new expedition to \\"Forbidden Valley\\" (in search of salad or dinos, whatever).<br />Will Tuck and TJ ever get married, or will they wind up in jail for all the havoc caused by their capture and mishandling of Gwangi?<br />What are they going to do with a giant rotting dinosaur carcass?<br /><br />Okay, I am thinking WAY too much for this film.<br /><br />Films of this style aren't big on logic or reason, they are meant to thrill and entertain the audience without requiring too much thought. In this respect, the film stands up to its contemporaries. However, it is a bit disappointing because the script has a good backstory and characters, and with a little more effort (or perhaps courage) on the part of the studio and writers it could have been a pioneering film, even one with a message.<br /><br />Instead, it succumbs to the formula method and fails its potential. Nevertheless, it is a fun film, a bit above the others of its kind, with fun performances by the actors.does deliver the charm.<br /><br />Of the animated characters, Gwangi is by far the most alluring, if voracious. Nevertheless, it is a fun film, a bit above the others of its kind, with fun performances by the actors.

0891419063|5.0|The content of this book defies description.  At numerous occasions while reading this memoir I had to stop and remind myself that all of these things truly happened.  When combat veterans talk about the inability of noncombatants to grasp war, I believe I have a slightly greater appreciation for what they mean.  I noticed that my mind tended to lapse into the book like any other story of fiction, except the indescribable horror, blackness, and utter hellishness that Mr. Sledge describes was all too real to him and the front line men he served with whose blood, viscera, and innocence stained the corral and mud of the many islands they fought on.  His descriptions in the book are so strong and unforgettable that for very brief moments of time the fog of fiction that clouded my mind was lifted and I found myself shocked beyond words.  For example, one passage that evoked in me a great feeling of sadness and remorse for the innocence these men left behind on our behalf came when Sledge stared down at a Marine corpse, he says:<br /><br />\\"Every time I looked over the edge of that foxhole down into that crater, the half-gone face leered up at me with a sardonic grin.  It was as though he was mocking our pitiful efforts to hang on to life in the face of the constant violent death that had cut him down.  Or maybe he was mocking the folly of war itself: \\"I am the harvest of man's stupidity.  I am the fruit of the holocaust.  I prayed like you to survive, but look at me now.  It is over for us who are dead, but you must struggle, and will carry the memories all your life.  People back home will wonder why you can't forget\\"\\".<br /><br />We who have never fought and tasted the bitter essence of war can never know, and thank God for that.  But if you want perhaps the slightest inkling of why Sledge and men like him can't forget, read this book.  Just read this book.

B0071WQ7WU|4.0|\\"Don't be delicate... be vast and brilliant\\" says the booklet to Shinedown's fourth album, Amaryllis. And the album does come close to brilliance. A band whose success rides on being accessible for radio and the casual listener, Shinedown have managed to create their own sound without acquiring any rough edges. Sure, there's the unnecessary profanity in \\"Nowhere Kids\\" and \\"My Name (Wearing Me Out),\\" for example, but the songs themselves are coherent, intelligent, perfectly executed and produced.<br /><br />Coming after the highly successful album The Sound of Madness and \\"Diamond Eyes\\" single, Amaryllis is undeniable evidence that Shinedown have found their niche. Shinedown always had a knack for writing both hard rockers (\\"Fly from the Inside,\\" \\"Heroes,\\" etc.) and more emotional songs (\\"45,\\" \\"Beyond the Sun,\\" etc.), but now they make great songs consistently with very few missteps. Unlike most modern rock groups, their ballads and mid-tempo songs always have tolerable, if not good, lyrics. The rock songs are always very strong, as well, and everything is carried through by fabulous vocalist Brent Smith.<br /><br />Shinedown's debut had some great songs, but its weakness was the bleak atmosphere contained throughout. Us and Them shed some light lyrically, and then The Sound of Madness was practically a rebirth. The fabulous sound of The Sound of Madness (partially due to Rob Cavallo, no doubt) remains entirely intact on Amaryllis, and it has been slightly improved. Critics of Shinedown have complained (probably without having heard \\"Fly from the Inside\\" or \\"Lady So Divine\\") about lack of interesting instrumentals  - Amaryllis will likely silence them. Lyrically, the songs are general enough for individual interpretation, with enough emotion and honesty to be interesting and worthwhile.<br /><br />\\"Adrenaline\\" is an exciting opening number followed by \\"Bully,\\" which basically dwarfs \\"Adrenaline.\\" \\"Amaryllis\\" is a perfectly placed ballad, followed by \\"Unity,\\" another great mid-tempo song - all of the first four songs contain guitar solos courtesy of Zach Myers; another highlight is his back-and-forth stomp and squeal on the next track, \\"Enemies.\\" Barry Kerch's drumming shines most in \\"Adrenaline\\" and \\"Bully,\\" and Eric Bass does a great job on bass throughout the album.<br /><br />The album may be a bit top-heavy, but it is better balanced than The Sound of Madness in terms of dynamics. As an added bonus, Wayne Bergeron can be heard screaming on his trumpet on \\"I'm Not Alright.\\" \\"Miracle\\" is a beautiful ballad and essentially the epitome of why Shinedown have so much pop appeal. \\"I'll Follow You\\" is a simplistic but effective number that sounds very much like Black Stone Cherry - a band with whom Shinedown toured while promoting The Sound of Madness. With the exception of the mediocre \\"For My Sake,\\" every song is fully formed and has a clear sense of purpose.<br /><br />Shinedown's fourth album proves without a doubt that The Sound of Madness was not a fluke. Amaryllis cements their place as one of the best rock bands today. Shinedown are only going to get bigger from here.<br /><br />Recommended: \\"Adrenaline,\\" \\"Bully,\\" \\"Miracle,\\" \\"I'll Follow You\\"<br /><br />4/5great mid-tempo song - all of the first four songs contain guitar solos courtesy of Zach Myers; another highlight is his back-and-forth stomp and squeal on the next track, \\"Enemies.\\" Barry Kerch's drumming shines most in \\"Adrenaline\\" and \\"Bully,\\" and Eric Bass does a great job on bass throughout the album.<br /><br />The album may be a bit top-heavy, but it is better balanced than The Sound of Madness in terms of dynamics. As an added bonus, Wayne Bergeron can be heard screaming on his trumpet on \\"I'm Not Alright.\\" \\"Miracle\\" is a beautiful ballad and essentially the epitome of why Shinedown have so much pop appeal. \\"I'll Follow You\\" is a simplistic but effective number that sounds very much like Black Stone Cherry - a band with whom Shinedown toured while promoting The Sound of Madness. With the exception of the mediocre \\"For My Sake,\\" every song is fully formed and has a clear sense of purpose.<br /><br />Shinedown's fourth album proves without a doubt that The Sound of Madness was not a fluke. B002G1YPH0|4.0|Item shipped in two days as promised...<br />easily detachable FAN from cooling fins...<br />This unit sits 6.5 inches above the motherboad when installed...so make sure your case is wide enough.<br />I suggest you install your own ulta thin insulated washers in the theaded studs that mount to the underside plate.<br />The reason being is your motherboard may have \\"traces\\" that run by or under the fan mount studs... MY ASROCK Z77 Extreme 4 did, and since<br />there is always some vibration from the fan or other components (as little as it may be) I didn't want to take the chance of having metal studs directly touching and then wearing down the coating over the traces on the MB and then shorting something out.<br />The protective film on the new studs was very thin and I turned a stud in one direction too far and the protective film came loose & off the stud.<br />So I replaced that with isulated washers for motherboards on all four studs. B00687XO1G|5.0|Recently I swallowed hard and paid Criterion's price for a Blu-ray disc of \\"Anatomy of a Murder\\" (1959) which has long been one of my favorite films.  I think I've watched it 20 or 30 times since I saw it in a theater in 1959.  Reminds me of one of George S. Kaufman's barbs: \\"What's the matter? Didn't you get it the first time?\\"  At any rate, the Blu-ray is terrific and we see it the way it was originally photographed and not the damned 4 x 3 screen with which Columbia/TriStar butchered the DVD release.<br /><br />It's among my favorite courtroom dramas along with \\"Inherit the Wind\\" and \\"Judgment at Nurmenberg.\\"<br /><br />The choice of Duke Ellington and his Orchestra for a music score was interesting.  Big bands were in decline since the end of World War II as many of the musicians of the day had been drafted and the rise of television did the rest. People at one time liked to go out at night and dance or go to the movies and now they were sitting at home watching their new TVs.  Ellington and Count Basie were the last two black bands still in the business but by the early 50s Ellington was ready to pull the plug.  The era of his greatest popularity had been the 30s and 40s.  Then something unexpected happened.  George Wein who was managing the Newport Jazz Festival booked Ellington's band for one night at the 1956 festival.  The evening went well -- they were graciously received but nothing special.  Then during a break Ellington decided to play two pieces he had written in the 1930s:  \\"Diminuendo in Blue\\" followed by \\"Crescendo in Blue,\\" connected by a saxophone solo by tenor saxophonist Paul Gonsalves.  The first part was played and Gonssalves started his rocking, blues-based solo.  After six or seven choruses, Ellington sensed a reaction coming from the audience so he signalled Gonsalves to continue playing.  The crowd became more and more excited.  By the time Gonsalves had blown 27 choruses, the audience was on the verge of a riot.  People were standing on the chairs and dancing in the aisles.  When the piece ended, Elllington began to fear an actual riot and  wanted to end the set.  George Wein told Ellington to play a couple of slow numbers to calm the audience down.  George Avakian of Columbia records was there and immediately put Ellington under contract and released an (edited) album of the concert.  It became a best-selling LP, unheard of for jazz.  Ellington's picture appeared on the cover of Time and he changed his mind about terminating his orchestra.  Most of his musicians had been with him through the 30s and 40s and when he composed a new piece he did so with a specific musician in mind, something no other band leader had ever done.<br /><br />How can you reconcile the music of a black jazz band with a story of a murder trial in upper Michigan?  You really can't except that Otto Preminger, an immigrant from Vienna, thought of jazz as THE American music, something most Americans did not, being largely unhip.<br /><br />There are moments in the film when Ellington's music seems to be at odds with the subject, but in the scene in which Stewart and Remick are talking outside of her trailer and he, as her attorney,  is warning her that her public behavior could well cause a negative outcome in her husband's trial, a soft bluesy theme with dense harmonies is heard and it is absolutely perfect.  We sense in his admonishment of Remick's behavior that he, himself, is drawn to her and it is all said with that musical interlude.<br /><br />The rendering of this great film on Blu-ray is ideal both in video and sound.  The spoken track is vividly recorded and very clear and Preminger used both very close and very distant microphoning to get different effects.  The music track, unlike the way jazz is usually recorded -- rather up-front and brilliant -- here is slightly soft in the treble, but not muffled, giving it an intimate quality ideal for this occasion.  Unfortunately there is no commentary track, but the extras are very fine and informative.

B001P9EQH8|4.0|The Fiio E5 is not meant to be an audiophile grade amplifier, rather a cheap solution for those looking for a little more power. I use my Fiio with my set of 250 ohm Beyerdynamic DT770 headphones when using a portable media player, as most do not provide the power needed to drive my headphones. The E5 does increase the power so that I can listen at a satisfactory level, and I was surprised to find that there was not much sacrifice in terms of sound quality. The bass boost feature, which I expected to be more of a marketing ploy rather than a useful tool, turned out to add a significant amount of bass and warmth. The reason why the device loses a star is because at loud volumes, it does detract from sound quality slightly. That being said, I would recommend this product to anyone looking for a cheap amplifier for high impedance headphones. For those looking for a better product in a higher price range, I would recommend looking at NuForce's UDACs.

0618001905|5.0|This book tells the shameful tale of Belgian colonization of the Congo, and the horror that resulted in the subsequent drive to extract wealth -- first ivory, then rubber, from that country. Despite the efforts of King Leopold of Belgium to whitewash events, they were discovered at the time, and public pressure did lead to changes in the early 20th century. However, in the aftermath of WWI perhaps, these crimes were forgotten.<br /><br />I think Hochschild set two goals for himself in writing this book, first to remind the world that this happened. In this he was totally successful. He manages to find ample evidence of the crimes that happened, and the commercial success of the book added this episode to the roster of crimes between nations. The chapter discussing Joseph Conrad is particularly interesting, showing that many of the nightmarish elements in [[ASIN:0486264645 Heart of Darkness]] were not metaphorical, but were rather straightforward descriptions of what was transpiring in the Congo at the time. The descriptions of the Americans and Europeans who came to realize what was happening in the Congo, and how they brought it to the attention of the world, reminds all of us of our potential to defeat great injustices against difficult odds.<br /><br />Hochsild also tries to pin the abuses in the Congo on King Leopold of Belgium, who thirsted for colonies and wealth. In this, he is less successful. Hochschild demonstrates that Leopold set the conquest in motion, and made demands that were ultimately met by agents who acted brutally, and then tried to hide evidence that this was happening as others came to report what they had seen. However, Hochschild paints Leopold as a Hitler-like murderer, and this is never convincing. It seems more likely that Leopold set a process in motion that had implications he hadn't anticipated, and that he made the brutal decision then not to reverse. Hochschild shows us Leopold trying to keep critics out of Congo, and trying to burnish the reputations of his agents there, but when he died, things didn't magically get better.<br /><br />I disliked Hochschild's definition of the human cost of Belgian occupation, which included \\"babies not born due to oppressive colonial conditions.\\" First, it seems like a lot of guesswork, and second, if you are going to criticize Belgium for the babies who weren't born because of their brutality, shouldn't you also give them credit for the babies that later *were* born, due to the transportation and communications infrastructure and medical technology, and cheap manufactured goods that they directly or indirectly made available? The population of Congo today is far greater than it could have been before the arrival of Europeans. Does this mean that King Leopold is actually a great humanitarian?sh the reputations of his agents there, but when he died, things didn't magically get better.<br /><br />I disliked Hochschild's definition of the human cost of Belgian occupation, which included \\"babies not born due to oppressive colonial conditions.\\" First, it seems like a lot of guesswork, and second, if you are going to criticize Belgium for the babies who weren't born because of their brutality, shouldn't you also give them credit for the babies that later *were* born, due to the transportation and communications infrastructure and medical technology, and cheap manufactured goods that they directly or indirectly made available? B007VTVGAQ|5.0|I'm rather ashamed to admit that, though I've always respected her obvious musical talent from afar, I'd somehow managed to escape Fiona's charms all these years...until the other night, when I couldn't resist any longer. After being intrigued enough by the samples I'd heard here and on her Myspace page, I purchased 'The Idler Wheel...' that same night, and proceeded to listen to it three times straight through. The first time, I was bewildered by what I was hearing, as it was difficult for me to connect with, in the same way it's difficult for me to connect with the avant-garde jazz of John Coltrane or Thelonious Monk, even though I could definitely appreciate the artistry involved in the music's construction. The second time it all made a little more sense, but I still wasn't totally feeling it. Toward the end of my third listen, however, I realized my cheeks were starting to hurt. I'd been smiling from ear to ear for about a good 30-minutes straight.<br /><br />This is an album that works so perfectly as a whole work of art, it's hard for me to cite specific highlights. Most of the songs are dependent on the ebbs and flows of what came before to have its maximum effect, so I definitely recommend listening to this album straight through a few times, or at least in large chunks, before deciding this \\"isn't your thing\\" (like some fans are saying). As a fan of more guitar-driven rock, it's not really my thing either, or so I thought. Then I just put my headphones on, let go of all my preconceived notions of what it \\"should\\" sound like, and let Fiona bring me into her world, a world that, to me, is almost like some weird, parallel-dimension 1940s in which she is the Billie Holiday of that reality, only one who plays a mean piano; with odd time-signatures, curlicue melodies, household object-based percussion, and insanely varied vocals and lyrics that make you feel her wide spectrum of emotions, even if you don't quite fully understand them.<br /><br />Fiona can go from singing about love, to anger, to sorrow, to despair, to paranoia, to neon zebras within the span of a couple songs, and the result is intoxicating, if you just give in to it and let her take you there. Her voice ranges from angelic to maniacal, and everything in between, sometimes all within the same song, leaving me feeling almost exhausted at times. But by the time I get to the excellent, kaleidoscopic (and insane) \\"Hot Knife,\\" which closes the album, I'm usually ready to take another ride. The production on the album is very sparse, with rarely more than piano and percussion framing Fiona's voice, but that only helps magnify the range and authenticity of emotion coming out of her, and the result is as close to perfection as I'm likely to hear this year, or any year.<br /><br />'The Idler Wheel...' is not always an easy, fun ride, but I can almost guarantee that, if you let it, it will be a fulfilling one. There's no higher compliment I can give than that. It's not impenetrable. Some works of art are just deeper and take longer to sink in, and those are the ones that tend to stick with you longer and affect you more profoundly. This album will one day be considered a masterpiece, I'm sure, but I'm just thankful to have finally discovered her beautiful work, even if I am about a decade and a half late. Hopefully she doesn't take another seven years to resurface next time, though thankfully I'll have a few new-to-me old albums of hers to tide me over in the meantime. And I'm sure that, if her next album is anywhere near as brilliant and inspiring as this one, the wait will definitely have been worth it.

B007I2BZIE|5.0|Veteran prog-rockers Rush (Geddy Lee, Alex Lifeson, Neil Peart) follow-up 2007's [[ASIN:B000NVIXFK Snakes & Arrows]] with their ambitious concept effort, 2012's highly anticipated [[ASIN:B007I2BZIE Clockwork Angels]].  Clockwork Angels has a weighty `concept' and storyline, but despite it's heady ambitions, Clockwork Angels easily delivers one of the year's strongest efforts.  This effort joins Van Halen and Jack White to deliver an intriguing rock effort in a time where rock itself seems `down.' Memorable, cerebral, and consistent, Clockwork Angels is superb.  Lee (vocals and bass) and Lifeson (guitars and keyboards) handle the musical composition while Peart (drums, percussion) handles the lyrics.<br /><br />\\"Caravan\\" opens the effort spirited, opening with bell sound effects and then introducing a rhythmic groove.  Lifeson's guitar have a superb timbre about them and are well mixed within the production.  Ever restless, \\"Caravan\\" features changes of pace instrumentally following the verse with different key, mood and tone; very creative.  Harmonically, the progression employed on the bridge section is superb.  The refrain adds to the programmatic conception: \\"...The caravan thunders onward/to the distant dream of the city/the caravan carries me onward/on my way at last/on my way at last...I can't stop thinking big...\\" The array of instrumental sounds plays as vital a role as the lyrics and Geddy Lee's vocals to \\"Caravan's\\" success.<br /><br />\\"BU2B,\\" released a couple of years ago, continues the mysterious, ambitious and complex nature of Clockwork Angels. Opening with ominous synthetic keyboard effects, soon Lifeson's acoustic guitar enters in.  Geddy Lee's vocals begin muted in effects, eventually repeated to their full glory: \\"I was brought up to believe/the universe has a plan/we are only human/it's not ours to understand.\\" Incorporating the Watchman, a figure within the narrative with the storyline, the heat further picks up within the effort.  The title track is a tour de force by all means, incorporating the use of various styles and sound effects.  Of the most interesting shifts, the incorporation of a blues, driven by Lifeson's acoustic guitar virtuosity, is sheer brilliance.  Peart's drumming facility and lyrics are as clever and vital as ever, particularly as Lee deliver's the refrain: \\"Clockwork angels, spread their arms and sing/synchronized and graceful, they move like living things/Goddesses of light, of Sea and Sky and Land/Clockwork Angels, the people raise their hands as if to fly...\\" The lyrics reference spiritual and the scientific, also exploring and conveying Peart's atheistic beliefs.<br /><br />\\"The Anarchist\\" continues to allure, opening with pummeling drums and dirty sounding guitars.  Lee's bass is noticeably present within the mix, which is yet another pro of the album as a whole.  Continuing to help their audience understand the crux of the narrative, programmatic notes are included within the jacket: \\"Walking among the people - who are so content, so blind - The Anarchist hears the pedlar's call and sneers derisively. \\"What do I lack?\\" Ah vengeance.\\" Definitely deep.  \\"Carnies\\" doesn't miss a beat, incorporating carnival sounds at the onset to contribute to the `tone poetic' nature.  Again, the lyrics in tandem with the overall production are the selling point.<br />\\"Halo Effect\\" is a more sensitive turn from the band, though a smart and successful contrast.  The program signifies love/relationships within this cut, and the use of strings and tamer sound confirms this.  Lee's lead vocals are clear as a bell.  Even the title references back to `angels' and further serves as a nuance of the tightly-sewn nature of the program.  \\"Seven Cities of Gold\\" gives the title track a `run' for it's money delivering perhaps the album's valedictory moment.  Overt from the onset, driven by Lee's bass and the employment of angular, muscular riffs, \\"Seven Cities of Gold\\" is nothing short of superb.  The bite andthe sheer resolve of the cut remind and reminisce back to [[ASIN:B000001ESF 2112]]'s \\"Passage to Bangkok.\\" Again, is it coincidence that the `seven,' a biblical and oft-cited religious number is central here?<br /><br />\\"The Wreckers\\" finds more play into Peart's atheistic agenda, with references to salvation, even if they be `indirect.' Within the program, the notes read: \\"...We seemed to find salvation in an unexpected signal light.  Steering toward it, we soon learned it was false...\\" Read into it as you may, it is no secret that Peart's lyrics have always been sharper and laden with messages deeper than what appears. \\"The Wreckers\\" shines brightly, even after following a juggernaut the likes of \\"Seven Cities of Gold.\\"<br /><br />\\"Headlong Flight\\" delivers superbly, joining top echelon cuts like \\"Clockwork Angels\\" and \\"Seven Cities of Gold.\\" Opening with a minimalistic groove and a driving, uptempo nature, \\"Headlong Flight\\" is Rush at its best. Angular, slick, virtuosic musicianship, \\"Headlong Flight\\" is CLASSIC.  \\"BU2B2\\" again focuses on more `realist' ambitions, as evidenced by it's program: \\"...No more boundless optimism, no more faith in greater powers, too much pain, too much grief, and too much disillusion.\\" Albeit brief, \\"BU2B2\\" is as much part of the totality of the project as anything else.<br /><br />\\"Wish Them Well\\" incorporates asymmetric meter brilliantly and Lee's `lungs' sound as impassioned as ever.  As good or possibly better is the culminating closer \\"The Garden,\\" which closes the epic, well epically.  Standing strongly upon the program the liner state: \\"I have now arrived at that point in my own story. There is a metaphorical garden in the acts and altitudes of a person's life, and the treasures of that garden are love and respect.\\"  Another spiritual reference, nonetheless.<br /><br />What else can be said? [[ASIN:B007I2BZIE Clockwork Angels]] is one of 2012's masterpieces.  For the prog-rock fan dying to go back to its heydays in the 1970s, this is like `Heaven.' Well crafted, well executed, and as cerebral as any classic prog-rock album, Clockwork Angels is excellent.the sheer resolve of the cut remind and reminisce back to [[ASIN:B000001ESF 2112]]'s \\"Passage to Bangkok.\\" Again, is it coincidence that the `seven,' a biblical and oft-cited religious number is central here?<br /><br />\\"The Wreckers\\" finds more play into Peart's atheistic agenda, with references to salvation, even if they be `indirect.' 0061692042|5.0|See the USA in your Chevrolet, but take a Ford to Canada<br /> Richard Ford's Canada (2012) is a marvel of construction, consisting of two loosely related parts punctuated by unexpected violent action (though nothing in the fiction of Richard Ford is entirely \\"unexpected,\\" thanks to his canny foreshadowing: his favorite quotation seems to be from John Ruskin: \\"composition is the arrangement of unequal things\\").  A third \\"part\\" offers an epilogue in hindsight that attempts to complete the story.  Part one tells the story of two twin siblings separated after their parents decide to rob a bank; both of them leave the town of Great Falls before they can be placed in an orphanage; the sister goes to San Francisco and becomes a sort of hippie; the brother is taken by a friend of his mother's to live across the border in Canada.  Part two of the novel is a coming-of-age story involving the boy, Dell Parsons, who unwittingly becomes involved in a murder plot.  But all he wants is to be educated, as he finally is, in the next province to the east, Manitoba.<br /> The boy, Dell, is the narrator (or at least the focal point:  the story is told from his perspective, fifty years after the events recounted, which gives him the perspective to reflect on meanings he could not have understood as a fifteen-year-old, a typical Fordian fictive trick).  Dell knows he is an American, but he learns to think and speak like a Canadian, and the novel forms a wonderful meditration on those differences.  The truly \\"bad\\" characters seem to be American, reacting out of fear and stupidity (Dell's parents, e.g.) or paranoia and selfishness (the mysterious ex-pat who rules the roost at the Leonard Hotel in Fort Royal, Saskatchewan).  The Canadians tend to be less impulsive, more restrained, and even, for the most part, more decent.  But the overview is not so simplistic.  This seemed to me the best Ford vehicle I had encountered since The Sportswriter.  --Jim Welsh, Salisbury U (Maryland)

There's no faking it here.<br /><br />Any album that I want to listen straight through, instead of throwing a couple of songs on my playlist, is a winner for me, and Born and Raised is one of those. If I had to pick a few top songs, I'd say \\"Queen of California\\", which is a great opener; the title track \\"Born and Raised\\" which feels like a follow-up to \\"Stop This Train\\" from the Continuum album--a wistful look at getting older; and \\"Walt Grace's Submarine Test, January 1967\\", which crafts a folk tale of accomplishing the impossible, which would make a great movie, and condenses it into one song.<br /><br />The album has a subtlety to it that makes each listen more enjoyable, so if you're iffy on your first listen, give it another try! Continuum is still my favorite John Mayer album, but after a few listens, Born and Raised has achieved a close second.

B007U1FEJE|5.0|What can I say that hasn't been said already.  This latest project took me by surprise and in a very good way.  From the opening track (clocking in at around 90 seconds) I knew that this 45 minute journey to surf, sun, and my fleeting youth would a special one but what I wasn't prepared for was the emotional connection I would develop with the songs.  This CD is a reflection of a the past and a reminder that life goes by in the blink of an eye.  Songs like \\"Isn't it Time\\", \\"Daybreak Over The Ocean\\" and \\"Beaches In Mind\\" remind me of those summer nights in my youth and thoughts of the girl that was such a part of those memories.  As time drifts people apart and we start to contemplate on life, the closing three songs on this CD are hauntingly beautiful.  They sum up a life that is closer to the end than the beginning and those warm memories of what seems like a lifetime ago are still present and fondly remembered.  If this is The Beach Boys final swan song, then I can't think of a better way to go out than with this CD as a bookmark and a tribute to a brilliant 50 year career. Buy this CD for yourself...and buy one for you Mom and Dad.

B0000009NQ|5.0|The Heroes album was recorded in Berlin in 1977, above Low and below Lodger. With Bowie for the Low and Heroes sessions was his trusted producer Tony Visconti; along with Brian Eno, whose ambient music on Another Green World and Discreet Music Bowie admired. It was a time of turmoil for Bowie personally, perhaps his lowest ebb was around the time of the appropriately titled, Low. His marriage with Angie was breaking up, causing so much stress that he believed he was having a heart attack and went to the hospital. He was having trouble with his management, and was accusing everyone around him of only being interested in him for his money.<br /><br />Iggy Pop was also part of Bowie's Berlin entourage, but Bowie was producing him, and that collaboration became The Idiot and Lust for Life. The bass and drums for Iggy's band were played by Hunt and Tony Sales, sons of TV pioneer Soupy Sales. The riff they laid down for Lust for Life was solid gold, and would later be used as the theme music for a cruise line commercial campaign, though sans any trace of Iggy's borderline obscene lyrics. The Sales Brothers would also later form the backbone of Bowie's least commercial outfit, Tin Machine. Bowie and Iggy were no doubt carousing like only they could. Iggy does recall, however, going into Berlin piano lounges and singing jazz standards like The Shadow of Your Smile. For a punk rocker, he always did have a golden baritone voice and would have made one hell of a crooner. In spite of--or perhaps because of--all the turmoil, it was a very productive time resulting in 4 great albums: 2 for Iggy and 2 for Bowie. China Girl from Iggy's The Idiot would later be covered by Bowie and become a hit, providing the Iggster with some much needed royalty checks in his autumn years.<br /><br />Low established a format that was followed by Heroes whereby the first half was somewhat more conventional \\"rock\\" songs with Bowie's vocals, while the second side was mostly instrumental, with Eno's treatments, tape loops, and ambient sounds. The title track of Heroes combined the two approaches, but more about that later. Low did open with the instrumental \\"Speed of Life\\" while Heroes ended with the sung \\"Secret Life of Arabia\\" but for the most part, the two sides were as different as night and day. This was back in the day when records were large, fragile, vinyl disks that had to be physically turned over when one side finished, and the needle had to be carefully placed on the rim, where it slid down a spiral groove, like a skier on a slalom course, or an ice cube on a skillet riding to its own melting.<br /><br />1977 was the year punk rock surfaced, as a reaction to the corporate rock produced by such bands as Fleetwood Mac, Journey, and Peter Frampton. Stadium tours were the order of the day with soaring ticket prices emphasizing the gap between audience and performers. Rock had become a commodity, it was big business, and had lost its edge and any sense of rebellion.<br /><br />With Low and Heroes Bowie was at least doing something not expected of him. For once he hadn't created a mask to hide behind. There were no outlandish characters like Major Tom, Ziggy Stardust, Aladdin Sane, or the Thin White Duke, throwing darts in his lover's eyes. If he was not topping the charts, at least he wasn't embarrassing himself and selling out.<br /><br />The 70s were a decade of fragmentation, and Bowie was an Artist of fragmentation. If his lyrics seemed to be coming out too literal, he would cut them up, using techniques culled from William S. Burroughs and his associate, Brion Gysin. Eno also employed Oblique Strategies, a series of cards he created that would suggest ways of working. Chosen at random, they might say, for instance, play something on an instrument other than your usual one. The I Ching, the Book of Changes, was also utilized. It is my contention that by using these methods Bowie opened himself up to a kind of power of prophecy that manifested itself inseemingly random lyrics that spoke directly to the listener about something personal. Has Bowie been reading my mail? There were other uncanny instances where Bowie would seem to predict future events. He was known to be heavily involved in the occult around this time. The ideas of Carl Jung, who also studied the I Ching, and proposed theories such as synchronicity, would seem to be in effect.<br /><br />Facism was a fear and concern of David, almost an obsession, though I would not go so far as to say that he was a fascist, as some had accused. Bowie thrived in the Berlin milieu, in spite or because of his personal turmoil. Bowie was working on a bio pic of the  painter, Egon Schiele, though it never came to fruition. The proposed title, Wally, probably didn't thrill anyone at the pitch meeting. The Berlin Wall, which had yet to fall at that time but would soon, was an imposing edifice, and a reminder of WW II and The Cold War. Echoes of the stories of Christopher Isherwood, which formed the basis for the musical, Cabaret, could still be heard in the German Beer Halls. All this, and the music of Kraftwerk and NEU! were all examples of the German influence being exerted on Bowie, though he exclaims in \\"Blackout\\" that \\"I'm under Japanese influence.\\"<br /><br />Actually, in the spirit of fragmentation, the music of Heroes combined German, Japanese, Arabian, world music, soul, blues, rock, disco, English music hall, the kitchen sink, and other influences. Let's look at the songs:<br /><br />\\"Beauty And The Beast\\" opens with some synthesizer growls, and then a beat ratchets up, tension builds, you hear a very long groan, and all heck breaks loose with a most unusual cacophonous sound. It is an amalgamation of various styles, but once you hear that voice you know that it has to be David Bowie's latest incarnation.<br /><br />\\"Joe The Lion\\" is next with another beast, this time a lion, but a lion who is just a regular guy named Joe. I wonder if he knows Joe The Plumber? Some speculate that Joe might also refer to Bowie's son, that he and Angie had named Zowie, but called Joe, once they realized what a cruel prank it was to name a boy Zowie. He later changed his name again to Duncan, so as not to be confused with Count Basie's drummer, and became a film director. But I digress... I myself wondered if Joe The Lion might refer to Joe Strummer, who sang a song called \\"1977\\" that had the line: \\"No Elvis, Beatles, or The Rolling Stones.\\" Since Heroes was recorded in Berlin in 1977, Bowie probably hadn't even heard of Strummer, but this could have been another example of Bowie's second sight, his gift for prophecy. After all, \\"after a couple of drinks on the house he was a fortune teller.\\"<br /><br />Joe the lion<br />Went to the bar<br />A couple of drinks on the house an' he said<br />\\"Tell you who you are<br />if you nail me to my car\\"<br /><br />The bit about \\"nail me to my car\\" is definitely a reference to Performance Artist Chris Burden of Venice, California, who had himself nailed to a Volkswagen in 1974. Another line in this song says \\"you get up and sleep\\" implying a reversal of waking and dreaming states. The music continues with the same rocking intensity of \\"Beauty and The Beast\\" but then there is a break that reminds me of the part in \\"Day In The Life\\" where they spliced in the part that McCartney wrote: \\"Woke up. Got out of bed. Dragged a comb across my head.\\" Anyway, it reminded me of that because it was an abrupt change but the two parts went together well, complimenting each other.<br /><br />\\"Heroes\\" the title track, is the 'hit single,' and it also reflects the Berlin setting from whence it came. Bowie even recorded it in German, along with a French version. It was a great ballad that harnessed waves of noise into a pleasing anthem. In his bio of Eno, David Sheppard described the process as \\"three oscillating VCS3 drones\\" from Eno, plus triumphal arcs of guitar [by Robert Fripp] filtered through Eno's extensive treatments . . . to freight the song with its towering opulence.\\" From producer Tony Visconti's autobiography there is this little vignette, where Tony went for a walk with his lover and kissed her by the Berlin Wall, and Bowie later said that he had been looking out the window and saw them. Visconti was very proud to have made such a Hitchcockian cameo in one of Bowie's most significant songs.<br /><br />\\"Sons of The Silent Age\\" is even slower than \\"Heroes.\\" It is almost a dirge, though not without its charms. Though the lyrics are still kind of fragmented, it tells a more cohesive story, though that is not to say that it isn't vastly strange and creates an atmosphere of science fiction. Perhaps a science fiction film from the silent era, like Fritz Lang's Metropolis?<br /><br />Sons of the silent age<br />Stand on platforms<br />blank looks and note books<br />Sit in back rows<br />of city limits<br />Lay in bed coming<br />and going on easy terms<br />Sons of the silent age<br />Pace their rooms<br />like a cell's dimensions<br />Rise for a year or two<br />then make war<br />Search through their one inch thoughts<br />Then decide it couldn't be done<br /><br />They also listen to tracks by Sam Therapy and King Dice, and if you ever need a name for a band, and all of the good ones by William S. Burroughs are already taken, then these two are available.<br /><br />\\"Blackout\\" might be a personal reference to the time earlier in 1977 that Bowie thought he was having a heart attack and was rushed to the hospital, or it could have been about numerous blackouts, things that Bowie did but has no recollection of ever doing. He actually showed up at The Grammys one year to give the award for the best female soul singer, spoke in French decked out in a fabulous tuxedo, gave a flawless presentation, and then had no recollection of being there, even when they showed him a video the next day in his management's office. In Le Freak, an autobiography of Nile Rodgers, who produced Let's Dance, and was no stranger to blackouts of his own, Nile asked Bowie about the Pin Ups sessions and he confessed that he had no memory of those sessions, or vast slabs of his past. The 70s for Bowie was like that saying old hippies had: \\"If you remember the 60s (or the 70s, especially the 70s) you weren't there.\\"<br /><br />Too, too high a price<br />To drink rotting wine from your hands<br />Your fearful hands<br /><br />And so concludes side one of Heroes by David Bowie.<br /><br />\\"V-2 Schneider,\\" the opener of side 2, combines the name of a German missile from WW II with the name of one of the guys from Kraftwerk. It could be an homage to the German inventors or techno, but at the very least, repeating the title over and over as the sole lyric, it gives off more of that German aura that pervades Heroes. There is also a horn section, and the drums give off a martial, marching beat, matched with percussive sound effects and droning sounds like rockets, missiles, V-2s, perhaps?<br /><br />\\"Sense of Doubt\\" is what I feel when I try to remember this song. I would listen to it again, to jar my memory, but it is way past my bedtime. If this song made the least impression, of all the ambient songs, then the next one more than made up for it.<br /><br />\\"Moss Garden\\" is for me the track that made the biggest impression of all of the ambient pieces on side 2. It reminds me of a hot summer day, so hot that you grow drowsy and fall asleep. You have nodded off under a tree. You can see sheets of hot air shimmering like a mirage of an oasis in a dry and barren stretch of parched sand. Maybe that's too dry a simile, because the title is \\"Moss Garden\\" but if that is the case, you are meditating in this zen garden on a summer's day. You hear distant sounds of crop dusters flying overhead. A dog barks, but it is far away, and doesn't disturb your dream. There is the sound of a gong being struck, but the tape is slowed down so much that you can barely recognize it. Bowie plays akoto over this backdrop, and you wonder if this is more of the Japanese Influence mentioned in \\"Blackout.\\"<br /><br />\\"Neuk&ouml;lln\\" is a ponderous and slow piece, with some saxophone parts, played by Bowie, and processed by Eno. As a saxophone player myself, I have to say that Bowie isn't playing that great, but he gets some pretty anguished cries out of his horn, and they are put to good use in the context of this piece. Neuk&ouml;lln is the eighth borough of Berlin, located in the southeastern part of the city. This piece is said by Bowie to be inspired by the Turkish ghetto there, and the despair of the Turks who lived there. The title also seems to be a reference to the German band NEU! formed by Klaus Dinger and Michael Rother after their split from Kraftwerk in the early 1970s. Though they never attained much commercial success NEU! is cited by Peter Doggett as a big influence on this project in his song-by-song analysis of Bowie's music in the 70s, The Man Who Sold the World.<br /><br />\\"The Secret Life of Arabia\\" ends Heroes appropriately with another vocal song detailing Bowie's upcoming middle eastern adventure across the sands of Arabia. As the electronically produced hand claps accent an Arabian beat, I can almost see Peter O'Toole as Lawrence, checking his profile in the reflection of an Arabian scimitar. Not sure exactly what it is about, after all, it is a secret, but Bowie gets credit for being prescient once again, as the Middle East would heat up even more in the years to come. Peter Doggett, in typical Doggett style, complains that Bowie pronounces \\"Arabia\\" with a cockney accent as bad as the one featured by Dick Van Dyke in Marry Poppins but I find it another charming example of Bowie's vocal eccentricity. In fact, I like this song a lot. Of the uptempo \\"rock\\" songs on Heroes, this one is my favorite, and a fitting end to a fine album.<br /><br />[[ASIN:0007229453 Tony Visconti: The Autobiography: Bowie, Bolan and the Brooklyn Boy]] by Tony Visconti<br /><br />[[ASIN:0062024655 The Man Who Sold the World: David Bowie and the 1970s]] by Peter Doggett<br /><br />[[ASIN:0753510022 Strange Fascination: David Bowie: The Definitive Story]] by David Buckley<br /><br />[[ASIN:0393065677 The Red Book]] by C. Visconti was very proud to have made such a Hitchcockian cameo in one of Bowie's most significant songs.<br /><br />\\"Sons of The Silent Age\\" is even slower than \\"Heroes.\\" It is almost a dirge, though not without its charms. Though the lyrics are still kind of fragmented, it tells a more cohesive story, though that is not to say that it isn't vastly strange and creates an atmosphere of science fiction. Perhaps a science fiction film from the silent era, like Fritz Lang's Metropolis?<br /><br />Sons of the silent age<br />Stand on platforms<br />blank looks and note books<br />Sit in back rows<br />of city limits<br />Lay in bed coming<br />and going on easy terms<br />Sons of the silent age<br />Pace their rooms<br />like a cell's dimensions<br />Rise for a year or two<br />then make war<br />Search through their one inch thoughts<br />Then decide it couldn't be done<br /><br />They also listen to tracks by Sam Therapy and King Dice, and if you ever need a name for a band, and all of the good ones by William S. Burroughs are already taken, then these two are available.<br /><br />\\"Blackout\\" might be a personal reference to the time earlier in 1977 that Bowie thought he was having a heart attack and was rushed to the hospital, or it could have been about numerous blackouts, things that Bowie did but has no recollection of ever doing. He actually showed up at The Grammys one year to give the award for the best female soul singer, spoke in French decked out in a fabulous tuxedo, gave a flawless presentation, and then had no recollection of being there, even when they showed him a video the next day in his management's office. In Le Freak, an autobiography of Nile Rodgers, who produced Let'sDance, and was no stranger to blackouts of his own, Nile asked Bowie about the Pin Ups sessions and he confessed that he had no memory of those sessions, or vast slabs of his past. The 70s for Bowie was like that saying old hippies had: \\"If you remember the 60s (or the 70s, especially the 70s) you weren't there.\\"<br /><br />Too, too high a price<br />To drink rotting wine from your hands<br />Your fearful hands<br /><br />And so concludes side one of Heroes by David Bowie.<br /><br />\\"V-2 Schneider,\\" the opener of side 2, combines the name of a German missile from WW II with the name of one of the guys from Kraftwerk. It could be an homage to the German inventors or techno, but at the very least, repeating the title over and over as the sole lyric, it gives off more of that German aura that pervades Heroes. There is also a horn section, and the drums give off a martial, marching beat, matched with percussive sound effects and droning sounds like rockets, missiles, V-2s, perhaps?<br /><br />\\"Sense of Doubt\\" is what I feel when I try to remember this song. I would listen to it again, to jar my memory, but it is way past my bedtime. If this song made the least impression, of all the ambient songs, then the next one more than made up for it.<br /><br />\\"Moss Garden\\" is for me the track that made the biggest impression of all of the ambient pieces on side 2. It reminds me of a hot summer day, so hot that you grow drowsy and fall asleep. You have nodded off under a tree. You can see sheets of hot air shimmering like a mirage of an oasis in a dry and barren stretch of parched sand. Maybe that's too dry a simile, because the title is \\"Moss Garden\\" but if that is the case, you are meditating in this zen garden on a summer's day. You hear distant sounds of crop dusters flying overhead. A dog barks, but it is far away, and doesn't disturb your dream. There is the sound of a gong being struck, but the tape is slowed down so much that you can barely recognize it. Bowie plays akoto over this backdrop, and you wonder if this is more of the Japanese Influence mentioned in \\"Blackout.\\"<br /><br />\\"Neuklln\\" is a ponderous and slow piece, with some saxophone parts, played by Bowie, and processed by Eno. As a saxophone player myself, I have to say that Bowie isn't playing that great, but he gets some pretty anguished cries out of his horn, and they are put to good use in the context of this piece. Neuklln is the eighth borough of Berlin, located in the southeastern part of the city. This piece is said by Bowie to be inspired by the Turkish ghetto there, and the despair of the Turks who lived there. The title also seems to be a reference to the German band NEU! formed by Klaus Dinger and Michael Rother after their split from Kraftwerk in the early 1970s. Though they never attained much commercial success NEU! is cited by Peter Doggett as a big influence on this project in his song-by-song analysis of Bowie's music in the 70s, The Man Who Sold the World.<br /><br />\\"The Secret Life of Arabia\\" ends Heroes appropriately with another vocal song detailing Bowie's upcoming middle eastern adventure across the sands of Arabia. As the electronically produced hand claps accent an Arabian beat, I can almost see Peter O'Toole as Lawrence, checking his profile in the reflection of an Arabian scimitar. Not sure exactly what it is about, after all, it is a secret, but Bowie gets credit for being prescient once again, as the Middle East would heat up even more in the years to come. Peter Doggett, in typical Doggett style, complains that Bowie pronounces \\"Arabia\\" with a cockney accent as bad as the one featured by Dick Van Dyke in Marry Poppins but I find it another charming example of Bowie's vocal eccentricity. In fact, I like this song a lot. Of the uptempo \\"rock\\" songs on Heroes, this one is my favorite, and a fitting end to a fine album.<br /><br />[[ASIN:0007229453 Tony Visconti: The Autobiography: Bowie, Bolan and the Brooklyn Boy]] by Tony Visconti<br/><br />[[ASIN:0062024655 The Man Who Sold the World: David Bowie and the 1970s]] by Peter Doggett<br /><br />[[ASIN:0753510022 Strange Fascination: David Bowie: The Definitive Story]] by David Buckley<br /><br />[[ASIN:0393065677 The Red Book]] by C. B007ZKY7XO|4.0|Scissor Sisters are a conundrum. When they debuted in 2004 they were an odd mix of 70's era Elton John meets modern dance pop sensibilities. They even covered Pink Floyd's \\"Comfortably Numb\\" in an unusual manner. Also, on their first two albums they were reminiscent of 70's era Bee Gees, particularly on 2006's \\"Ta-Dah\\" with \\"Don't Feel Like Dancin'\\". So, all of this led me to wonder if Scissor Sisters had any originality. Were they going to continue as a pastiche, or were they going to go their separate way.<br /><br />Yes and no. 2010's \\"Night Work\\" was an interesting departure into dance music, but they still were unable to escape the fact that they had not broken new ground. And now comes \\"Magic Hour\\". It took me a few listens to get into this album, but once I got into its groove it made sense to me. Again, the band can't seem to find its way to new heights in terms of originality, but they do a fine job of mixing things up.<br /><br />\\"Magic Hour\\" has variety going for it, as well as melodies. \\"Baby Come Home\\", \\"Keep Your Shoes On\\"--these two jump start the album, giving listeners melodies and hooks. \\"Inevitable\\" is a ballad that maybe should have been left off. \\"Only The Horses\\" is one of the best tracks here, and is easily a dance floor anthem not unlike \\"Fire With Fire\\" from their last album. \\"Year Of Living Dangerously\\" is interesting lyrically but packaged in a rather dull melody line, and probably should have been left off. \\"Let's Have A Kiki\\" is humorous and a side of Scissor Sisters we seldom hear, but they really should explore this side because this song demonstrates their uniqueness as a band. Same goes for \\"Shady Love\\". The rap/singing is something the band hasn't tried before and it works quite well within the confines of a dance song. The other great track here is the Spanish flavored \\"San Luis Obispo\\" with its irresistible melodic hook and it's hopeful, yearning lyrics, the song is quite a departure for the band and they should have tried this approach more here because they really pull it off.<br /><br />\\"Self Control\\" is a droning dance electronic track that is fine, and \\"Best In Me\\" is another catchy track. I agree with most of the reviewers who pointed out that \\"The Secret Life Of Letters\\" is the worst song here. It goes absolutely nowhere. They should have left it off. Album closer \\"Somewhere\\" is another solid track with more soul searching. All in all, of the 12 tracks, the only dud is \\"The Secret Life Of Letters\\". \\"F--- Yeah\\" is okay for a bonus dance cut. The two remixes and the brief message from Ana Matronic, were, in my not so humble opinion, a complete waste of space and time.<br /><br />All this being said, I have to give more criticism. Scissor Sisters burst onto the scene in 2004 with a re-packaging of a bygone era of music, and when I listened to that debut album again, along with their other albums, I was reminded that the band still have work to do. I like them, but I wouldn't say they're one of my favorites because they continue to walk the side of the fence of playing it safe. This band has so much potential to break out of the cult following and into the mainstream, yet they just miss the mark with each release. Back in the day, a band could take up to 10 albums before finally maturing into a great band and finding their identity in the musical world--just listen to Hall & Oates, Rush, Inxs, U2, Blondie, and yes, even The Beatles and The Beach Boys.<br /><br />So my hope is this: I want the band to go back to the drawing board and pretend like their record company has given them an ultimatum--either deliver a hit album or we drop you from our label. I mean, if ABC could deliver the excellent and whimsical \\"How To Be A Zillionaire\\" back in 1985, Scissor Sisters can certainly do the same!<br /><br />Here's how \\"Magic Hour\\" compares to Scissor Sisters' previous works:<br /><br />2004 Scissor Sisters: Three and a Half Stars<br />2006 Ta-Dah: Four Stars<br />2010 Night Work: Four Stars<br />2012 Magic Hour: Four Stars2012 Magic Hour: Four Stars

0142000663|5.0|In this novel about Oklahoma farmers forced by the Dust Bowl and the Great Depression to seek a new life for themselves as migrant laborers in California, John Steinbeck may well have written the Great American Novel.  \\"The Grapes of Wrath\\" is the story of the Joad family, but it's also the story of a people on the move, a nation in crisis, and humanity in its extremes of greed and goodness.<br /><br />The first quarter of the novel tells of young Tom Joad's homecoming after several years in prison for killing a man in a drunken brawl.  Contact with his family has been minimal over the years, and he looks forward to seeing his parents, grandparents, and siblings again - but the house is empty, obviously abandoned, like so many others in this land where a combination of drought and poor agricultural techniques has resulted in failure and foreclosure on countless family farms.  Fortunately, Tom learns from a neighbor that his family has gone over to his uncle's place, and he arrives there just in time to join them on their way to California, where they've been told there's plenty of work in the state's lush Central Valley.<br /><br />The second quarter of the novel is the story of the Joads' arduous journey west on Route 66, a trip distinguished by breakdowns, death, and intimations by those who have been there that California may be something less than the paradise they've been led to imagine.  The final half of the novel follows the Joads after they arrive in California, only to discover that it's possible to starve even in a land of plenty as too many would-be workers are forced to compete for available jobs by accepting wages barely sufficient to buy enough food from one day to the next.  The novel ends with one of the most stunning and affecting scenes you'll ever read, and although nothing at all is resolved, the story feels complete.<br /><br />The structure of the novel underscores Steinbeck's creation of the Joads as the human face of a social crisis.  Long chapters that advance the plot alternate with short chapters in which the Joads are never mentioned, in which Steinbeck's richly poetic prose establish the physical and moral setting of his work: the conditions leading to the Dust Bowl, the loss of a way of life, the journey to a new beginning, and the disillusionment and growing anger of the migrants - all on a massive scale.  These short, poignant chapters are as beautiful, captivating, and necessary as the story chapters, as they provide context and grant a kind of holy universality to the Joads' experiences.<br /><br />Steinbeck's writing is raw, earthy, and viscerally powerful.  This is realism at its finest: full of small, telling details, and at times casually vulgar, not for shock value but because life itself is casually vulgar.  I was about 13 the first time I read this novel, and the blunt honesty of the writing was a bit much for my somewhat sheltered mind; I remember feeling uncomfortable when an old man reached into his pants and \\"contentedly scratched under the testicles,\\" as that wasn't a word I was used to seeing in print, at least outside of biology texts.  I loved the background chapters but found the Joad chapters distasteful for the first hundred pages or so, when I finally allowed the vivid immediacy of Steinbeck's style to make the characters real for me.  As an adult, I have no such difficulties and am able to appreciate the masterful style and rich characterizations immediately.  This is a mature novel, about people too crassly human to elicit our pity, but too warmly human not to elicit our compassion.<br /><br />I must admit that as a native Californian, I feel a special connection with this novel.  For most of my life I lived just a few blocks away from the old Route 66 (although farther west than the point where the Joads left it to go north).  Several of my husband's children live in the Central Valley, around places Steinbeck mentions by name.  However, Steinbeck's skill is such that even if you've never been there, you'll close this novel feeling as though you had.  This is a novel every American should read - indeed, everyone interested in what it means to be human in trying times.  These days more than ever we need this book, we need this reminder of the values of proud self-sufficiency and fierce decency, for it is when we stop pulling, and pulling together, that we lose our way.en if you've never been there, you'll close this novel feeling as though you had.  This is a novel every American should read - indeed, everyone interested in what it means to be human in trying times.  These days more than ever we need this book, we need this reminder of the values of proud self-sufficiency and fierce decency, for it is when we stop pulling, and pulling together, that we lose our way.

B000O77SRC|5.0|Whenever the holidays swing around and that good will toward men sentiment threatens to manifest, I sift thru my DVD collection for my favorite Christmas film: DIE friggin' HARD!! That's right. In my household, it's not MIRACLE ON 34TH STREET or IT'S A WONDERFUL LIFE or A CHRISTMAS STORY that gets copious playing time. Give me John McClane, barefoot and bloody exhausted and unappreciated, holed up in the Nakatomi building, trying hard to be that fly in the ointment and foil a twelve-man-crew of hi-tech terrorists. Give me that smartasss New York cop every time. Because John McClane, he earns his Christmas cheer.<br /><br />In 1988 DIE HARD founded an entire new action sub-genre. It influenced a slew of imitators: SPEED (DIE HARD on a bus), UNDER SIEGE (DIE HARD on a boat), SUDDEN DEATH (DIE HARD in a hockey arena), etc. DIE HARD propelled Bruce Willis to Hollywood's stratosphere. John McClane would only be the first of Willis' repertoire of lunch pail everyman heroes. Schwarzenegger, Stallone, Richard Gere, and Burt Reynolds have probably kicked themselves ad nauseam for turning down this part. Their loss, our gain. No other actor could've packed this role with so much humanity and vulnerability and wiseassery. Could you imagine Stallone trapped in Nakatomi Plaza? I shudder.<br /><br />I've got mad love for this film, so maybe that renders me blind to its faults, because I can't see any. There's such a strong human element to the storytelling. We observe the tension between McClane and his estranged wife Holly (Bonnie Bedelia). We note the world-weary camaraderie between McClane and his one L.A. cop ally, Sgt. Al Powell (Reginald VelJohnson). McClane and Al's talks via radio are only some of the fantastic grace notes strewn throughout the film. These are actors on top of their game. Even the ancillary characters feel three-dimensional (okay, maybe not the two gung-ho FBI guys). Alan Rickman, making his big screen debut, is pitch perfect as the big bad. The cat-and-mouse games enacted between his snotty Hans Gruber and the blue-collar McClane provide some of the film's most crowd-pleasing moments.<br /><br />Down the years DIE HARD has earned a well-deserved rep as a rousing non-stop action thriller. So it's all the more amazing that the first 20 minutes doesn't have any violence at all, or, in fact, anything ominous. Director John McTiernan takes the time to set things up, to develop John McClane as a full-blooded character. After those 20 minutes, when the fit hits the shan and that sense of urgency just escalates, we're gripped hard and totally on board.<br /><br />The sequels, while good, aren't quite as good because A) that sense of familiarity kicks in and B) the franchise kept expanding McClane's playground. Ensuing films went away from one of the original's strengths, which is one man trapped in a CONFINED SPACE with a bunch of killers. There is so much suspense in that premise. McTiernan's precision camera work establishes that sense of claustrophobic space and, in familiarizing the audience with its geography, elevates the Nakatomi building into a supporting character.<br /><br />DIE HARD brings us back to an age when our heroes didn't automatically throw down with the kung fu. John McClane, refreshingly, is a brawler, a bruiser, someone who wades in there swinging and maybe issuing a sneaky elbow or two. McClane is not indestructible (at least not in the first DIE HARD). He doesn't easily dispatch his assailants. He is taxed to his very limits. This makes him a much more compelling protagonist and it is hugely why those jeopardized moments crackle with such white-knuckle suspense. We easily identify with him. And I love his strokes of ingenuity, his knack for improvising under duress.<br /><br />And, omigod, he's so funny. John McClane is hilarious. The best thing about his humor is that it feels organic and unforced and off the cuff. Credit Willis for having ad-libbed most of those lines.<br /><br />So one badge, one unexpected guest, desperate and wounded, must outwit and outfight a professional crew of villains in a sealed-off highrise, even as thirty people, including his wife, are held hostage. And what part of that doesn't speak Christmas to you? If I had my druthers, some sweet day, quotes from DIE HARD will be freely bandied about during the holiday season, alongside the likes of \\"Bah, humbug!\\" and \\"Every time a bell rings an angel gets his wings.\\" That'll be the Christmas spirit evoked all proper, when some bloke drinking eggnog remarks: \\"We're gonna need some more FBI guys.\\" And to all a good night.<br /><br />This DVD's bonus stuff:<br /><br />- Audio Commentary by director John McTiernan and production designer Jackson DeGovia<br />- Scene Specific Audio Commentary by special effects supervisor Richard Edlund<br />- Sub-title Commentary by various crew members and actor Alan Rickman, as well as analysis by film journalist/historian Eric Lichtenfeld<br />- Branching version with the extended Nakatomi building power shutdown scene cut back ine unexpected guest, desperate and wounded, must outwit and outfight a professional crew of villains in a sealed-off highrise, even as thirty people, including his wife, are held hostage.
