0307719219|4.0|The virtuous circle works through several mechanisms. First, the logic of pluralistic political institutions makes usurpation of power by a dictator, a faction within the government, or even a well-meaning president much more difficult, as Franklin Roosevelt discovered when he tried to remove the checks on his power imposed by the Supreme Court, and as Sir Robert Walpole discovered when he attempted to summarily implement the Black Act. In both cases, concentrating power further in the hands of an individual or a narrow group would have started undermining the foundations of pluralistic, political institutions and the true measure of pluralism is precisely its ability resist such attempts.<br /><br />Pluralism also enshrines the notion of the rule of law, the principle that laws should be applied equally to everybody - something that is naturally impossible under an absolutist monarchy. But the rule of law, in turn, implies that laws cannot simply be used by one group to encroach upon the rights of another. What's more, the principle of the rule of law opens the door for greater participation in the political process and greater inclusivity, as it powerfully introduces the idea that people should be equal not only before law but also in the political system.<br /><br />Second, as we have seen several times before, inclusive political institutions support and are supported by inclusive economic institutions. This creates another mechanism of the virtuous  economic institutions remove the most egregious extractive economic relations, such as slavery and serfdom, reduce the importance of monopolies, and create a dynamic economy, all of which reduces the economic benefits that one can secure, at least in the short run, by usurping political power.<br /><br />Finally, inclusive political institutions allow a free media to flourish, and a free media often provides information about and mobilizes opposition to threats against inclusive institutions, as it did during the last quarter of the nineteenth century and first quarter of the twentieth century, when the increasing economic domination of the Robber Barrons was threatening the essence of inclusive economic institutions in the United States.<br />________________________________________________<br /><br />Synopsis: Political inclusive institutions (pluralism) support inclusive economic institutions (free markets). That's a good thing. Extractive institutions (dictatorships) use their power to enrich themselves at the expense of society. That's a bad thing.

0765316994|4.0|The philosopher Jean-Paul Sartre once said, on the meaning of life, \\"Man is nothing else but what he makes of himself.\\" Frederich Nietzche said, \\"If we possess a why of life, we can put up with almost any how.\\" And Stephen King wrote, \\"Life sucks, then you die.\\"<br /><br />It'll take a far better philosopher than I to really look at this book from an existentialist viewpoint, but I strongly suspect that it would be a lot of fun to do. After all, one of the major questions that philosophy - and existentialism in particular - tries to address is that of why we are here. What is our purpose in life? What, in the end, does it all mean? For us out here, that's a question we can't really know the answer to, and thus a whole branch of philosophy exists to tell us that it doesn't really matter. That maybe we don't have a purpose imposed upon us from outside, but that's okay. We can create our own. We can contribute our own verses to the powerful play of life, as Whitman would have it, and in the end we are responsible for our own lives.<br /><br />But what if we weren't? What if there was a being that orchestrated our lives, willing them into - and more importantly out of - existence? What would you do with the realization that your life is not entirely your own? And even worse, the realization that the person in control of it doesn't really care all that much about you?<br /><br />That is the problem faced by Ensign Andrew Dahl of the Universal Union flagship Intrepid. It is the 25th century, and things couldn't be better. He has a chance to see new world and new civilizations, to boldly go... Well, you know the rest. Dahl is at the frontier of science and exploration, and is determined to make the most of it.<br /><br />If he survives.<br /><br />Alone among the ships of the UU, the Intrepid loses crew at an alarming rate. Dahl soon discovers a fact that has been known for years by those crew members who are bright enough to spot the pattern: people who go on away missions with the command staff will, almost inevitably, die. Toxic gasses, killer machines, Borgovian land worms - these are just a tiny sampling of dangers that have done in ensigns and miscellaneous crew for years, and no one seems to know why. All they can do is make sure they're not in the room when the Captain comes in, looking for someone who pop down to a planet's surface to find out why that mining colony hasn't reported in recently.<br /><br />Dahl, of course, just can't let himself and his friends die, so he begins digging into the true nature of their lives on the starship Intrepid. What he discovers is a truth almost too mad to be believed: their lives are not their own. A greater power is directing events on the Intrepid, dictating who lives and who dies, and that greater power doesn't seem to be very good at what it does. So Dahl and his friends have to bet everything on the power of the Narrative, meet their makers and try to find a way to secure their freedom. Or, failing that, a way to see to it that their lives have more meaning than they had before.<br /><br />As always with John Scalzi, I recommend picking this up. It's a very fast read - I finished it in under a day - and it has the tight combination of humor, thoughtfulness, and genuine emotion that I have come to expect from his work. From a premise that is incredibly simple - \\"The crew of a starship realize they're doomed if they go on away missions and try to change their fate\\" - he's built up a multi-layered exploration into the meaning of life and death. The universe he's given to us is one where people are denied the ability to give meaning to their own lives, and have to rely one an unseen force to do it for them. The fight, then, is to acquire that ability to decide. To gain agency, as it were. They want to be able to control their own existence so badly that they risk their existence entirely.<br /><br />The corollary, then, is very simple: what are you doing with your life? We, the readers,have that agency. We can make decisions for our own lives and our own purposes. If we succeed or fail, we can do so knowing that we made those successes or failures possible. [1] In a sense, we don't know how good we have it, something that is brought up in the second of three codas to the main novel. We can choose. We can create meaning in our lives without hoping that some higher power will do it for us. So why don't we?<br /><br />For a book that presents itself as a quick, fun read, there are certainly layers upon layers of meaning in it that could be a lot of fun to explore. The only complaint, really, is that it wasn't long enough. And I don't mean that he skipped essential scenes, or that he should have opted for a Tolkien/Jordan/Martin-esque style of describing every goddamn thing that showed up on the page, but there were points where I just wanted him to slow down a bit and let us appreciate the moments for what they were. There's a scene in chapter 21, for example, that should be really emotional and meaningful, but it's almost entirely dialogue. Good dialogue, yes, but I wanted to linger over it a bit, and that's true for a lot of scenes in the book. Scalzi writes wonderful banter, and makes his characters sound real, but I want to see things as well as hear them.<br /><br />Also, to be honest, I expected the last page to just be a picture of Scalzi at his computer, turning to the camera and winking. It would have been hilariously meta, but I guess he's not as gimmicky as that.<br /><br />Buy the book and enjoy it. If you're a fan of Star Trek - which was, given the title, a huge inspiration for the story - you'll no doubt appreciate it more than most. Even if you haven't watched every episode of the original series, though, the Red Shirt character is one that has permeated all levels of fiction, and has died many times in order to advance the plots that you love so well. He even has one poor guy who's not only a Red Shirt, but nearly at the end of histour and about to get married. There was no way he'd survive. Take some time out for these poor, expendable bastards and give them a chance to shine.<br /><br />----<br />\\"The [Borgovian Land Worms] were in a frenzy. Somebody was now likely to die. It was likely to be ensign Davis.\\"<br />- from \\"Redshirts\\" by John Scalzi<br />----<br />[1] There are plenty of external, uncontrollable factors, of course, which can all be lumped together under the term \\"luck,\\" but you know what I mean.

0765326353|5.0|There are times when I hate having grown up to be a fantasy fan. Most of the time it's when I pick up a book that seems promising - maybe because it's from a familiar author, or because you heard from a friend of a friend that it was good - but it turns out to be disappointing. Stock characters, old and tired plotlines, and a world that's basically Tolkien with some greasepaint and false noses added on. Given the number of people who write fantasy, the odds of coming across a truly interesting world with compelling characters and a story that has some surprises is difficult indeed.<br /><br />Fortunately, it looks like Sanderson has managed to pull it off.<br /><br />The world of Roshar is a strange and tempestuous place. The seasons come and go in unpredictable ways, sometimes bringing with them great highstorms that are so powerful that even the plants of this world have evolved ways to hide from them. It is a world filled with spirits, ubiquitous beings called spren, which pop up for almost any reason. There are the spren of nature - windspren, firespren, rotspren, riverspren and the like. There are spren that seem attracted to humans, like alespren, gloryspren, anticipationspren and logicspren. No one really knows what they are or why they exist, but they are everywhere in this world.<br /><br />The greatest kingdom in Roshar is that of Alethkar, which is barely a nation at all. A loosely bound alliance of ten high princes, the people of Alethkar are a hostile, ambitious, violent folk whose first and greatest love is battle and winning. Since the assassination of their king by the savage Parshendi, they have been involved in a seemingly endless siege of revenge on the great Shattered Plains.<br /><br />The greatest warriors of Alethkar - or any nation - are those who wield the amazing shardblades. Swords that seem to condense out of mist, the shardblades can cut through anything, though if they cut through a person their effects are a little more subtle. A warrior armed with a shardblade, wearing shardplate armor, can use the incredible power of stormlight to achieve feats that no normal man could survive. Bound within glowing gemstones and restored by the howling winds of the highstorms, stormlight is Roshar's greatest treasure.<br /><br />Within this world we follow an ensemble cast which, while adhering to certain fantasy archetypes, still is made interesting and worth watching. Dalinar, the brother to the dead Alethi king, is searching for a way to hold together the weak nation that his brother formed. He has been learning of the old ways, the teachings of the vanished and reviled Knights Radiant, in the hopes that they can help hold his people together.<br /><br />On the other end of Alethi society is Kaladin. Once a promising young surgeon, Kaladin joined the army in hopes of being able to fight on the Shattered Plains. He made it there, but not as a soldier - as a member of a bridge crew, one of the most expendable resources in the entire war. He became the lowest of the low, forced to find a reason to stay alive.<br /><br />In a city far from the fighting, young Shallan Davar has fought to become the ward of the great heretic scholar Jasnah Kholin. While she has ostensibly come to learn from the woman, her true purpose is to steal Jasnah's soulcaster, a device which, if used properly, can turn something into something else - stone into smoke, glass into blood, a man into fire. With this, Shallan hopes to revive her family's flagging fortunes after the death of her father. What she discovers with Jasnah, of course, is far, far more.<br /><br />Then there's Szeth-son-son-Vallano, truthless of Shinovar. Poor, poor Szeth. From a race of people known for their peaceful and easygoing natures, Szeth is the most powerful assassin the world has seen. He can harness the stormlight to manipulate gravity, making him able to do the impossible while he uses his shardblade to cut down anyone in his way. In truth, though, Szeth wishes only one thing - to find someone who is good enough to kill him, and end his tormented life.<br /><br />As you may have guessed, it's a complicated tale, and Sanderson doesn't hold to this whole \\"Give the reader time to get used to it\\" style of writing. If you're not paying attention from the beginning, you are likely to be very, very lost within the first chapter or so. But once everything settles down, the story turns into a fast-paced, multi-leveled adventure that takes place in a world that is imaginative and fascinating.<br /><br />The characters are enthralling, too, with many levels and - most importantly - flaws. While Kaladin is a brilliant organizer and leader, he has to fight continually against the despair of realizing what his life has come to. The easy thing would be to allow himself to die, but he knows he can't let himself do that. Dalinar, plagued by visions of what might be Roshar's ancient past, is fighting centuries of Alethi martial tradition by trying to bring the high princes together and end the war, rather than allowing it to go on. He's pulled between the love of his nephew, the king, and his frustration that the king won't be strong enough to do what needs doing. Shallan, who left her home with a clear purpose, is finding that nothing was what she thought it would be. Jasnah isn't an evil woman, despite being a heretic, and her plan to steal the soulcaster becomes less and less certain the more she learns.<br /><br />All of these characters are at the front edge of thousands of years of history, much of it shrouded in uncertainty - legendary Knights Radiant who fought Voidbringers before giving up their duty and turning against mankind. What actually happened is unknown, and perhaps won't ever be known. But the effects of those events echo to the present day, causing problems which our characters will eventually have to deal with.<br /><br />Perhaps the greatest compliment I can give to Sanderson is that when I finished the book, I immediately went back to the first page and started reading again. There are very few books that have inspired me thus, but this one did - especially after the cascading Big Reveals at the end, which explain a lot, and cast a new light on a whole lot more.<br /><br />What's more, I found myself wishing that I had access to an animation studio while I read the action scenes. Fights can be hard to do in written form - there's a tendency to either describe too much or too little, and very often the reader gets slowed down trying to visualize what's happening in the story. Sanderson is very, very good at writing action, something I first noticed in Towers of Midnight. Even when Szeth is hopping from floor to ceiling to wall, flinging people around like toys, the action was very clear in my mind's eye, and it's something I would love to see animated, if not done in live action.<br /><br />And yes, to get back to why I hate being a fantasy reader sometimes, it is the first book in a series, which means I'm likely to be following it for quite some time. There's nothing truly wrong with that - there are plenty of series that I've followed in my day - but I never look forward to the waiting game that you have to play as the author works on the next book. To be fair, though, Sanderson is busy right now finishing up my favorite series, The Wheel of Time, so I think I can give him a little latitude.<br /><br />In any case, if you're looking for a dense, fun new series to read, definitely pick this up. I plan on getting into some of his other books, mainly in order to have something to do while I wait for the next one of these.<br /><br />--------------------------------------<br /><br />\\"The purpose of a storyteller is not to tell you how to think, but to give you questions to think upon. Too often, we forget that.\\"<br />- Hoid, The Way of Kings

0525478817|4.0|Having written a YA teen cancer/romance story, I was a bit nervous about this book. I was afraid it would hold all the secrets my own story contained. Upon finally cracking the story open, I was relieved to find that The Fault in Our Stars is not A Place In This Life... but something else entirely and very good. Hazel and Augustus are two kids with terminal cancer (at first Augustus seems totally cured, having endured amputation of his leg due to osteosarcoma) who meet in support group. They click almost instantly and with a charm and wit unlike any other teen couple I've read in recent years.<br /><br />They are also extremely mature with their language and dialogue, which I find unrealistic. However, there are kids (and adults) out there who use complex language more freely than others (think Frasier Crane), and Hazel and Augustus are in this realm. Once I got over the mature dialogue, I saw that John Green was able to weave a story around the friends and lovers that also involved cancer. At times I forgot that the kids were suffering, and that is wonderful because when you have a disease, be it cancer or something autoimmune or neurological, your life shouldn't be the disease. You still have to live. Hazel and Augustus do this.<br /><br />While many readers of this book cried, I did not. Perhaps it is because I've experienced the horrors of cancer in my family and in my own life (thought I had thyroid cancer several years ago, which is what Hazel had). Not sure why I didn't cry. Maybe I am numb?<br /><br />My only other qualm with this book is the ending. It felt stunted and unsatisfying. Now, I realize that with books pertaining to characters with cancer that the outcomes are often dismal and sad at best, and that is conveyed in this book. However, I would have envisioned a different ending. Maybe it's just me.<br /><br />Overall, this book was an easy read because it flowed to perfection. I enjoyed it immensely and will seek out more of Green's books in the future.ure.

B007VTVGFQ|5.0|An event Fiona diehards have grown used to, another album has finally been released.  We only had to wait a bit more than half a decade; but for someone who fought her record company (Sony....no small feat) in order to finally release \\"Extraordinary Machine,\\" nothing Fiona Apple does comes as any great surprise.<br /><br />When Apple exploded on the scene in the mid-nineties with a superb offering, \\"Tidal,\\" many felt they were in the direct line of fire of slaughter by brilliance.  They were halfway right:  While there is no denying the brilliance of Fiona Apple, her offerings have been sparce, highly-anticipated and....eventually, realized.  And so we have \\"The Idler Wheel.............\\"<br /><br />Rather than analyze each song, I will leave it at this:  If you are an Apple fan, buy it and check your area for her tour.  For the virgin, this might be a good place to start, although I don't know if Ms. Apple will EVER be able to best her effort that was \\"When the Pawn..........\\" It just might always be wise to start at the beginning.  On the other hand, if this is your first experience, listen and worry about the preceding efforts at a later time.<br /><br />When you listen to this album and the three that preceded, you are immediately aware of the smoky voice that is, indirectly, seducing you to search with her.  It's not hard to hear that Apple is influenced by the likes of the Sarah Vaughan, Dinah Washington and, to a lesser sense, the heroin-laced syrup that made Billie Holiday an icon, deserved or not.<br /><br />Fiona Apple, like a fine, single-malt scotch, is an acquired taste.  There is no commercialism evident, nor is her music tied to simple meter.  In other words, listening to Fiona Apple, while not requiring any formal music background, is a challenge that, for most, requires preparation.  However, her devoted group of fans cannot help but be delighted by \\"The Idler Wheel.....\\"  What we are listening to is a phenomenon that could just as easily be performing in Minton's, New York City, circa 1940-50.  That's how I prefer to think of her.<br /><br />If you decide to take the leap of faith, trust me and buy \\"When The Pawn.....\\" first.  Listen to it and decide whether you want to hear more.  If so, \\"The Idler Wheel....\\" will suit you just fine.  Just don't forget that \\"Tidal\\" and \\"Extraordinary Machine\\" are also begging for a listen

B00002DE4E|3.0|I found Joy Division via my room mate who had an extensive CD collection. Until then I was unaware of the connection between New Order and JD. My initial discovery was the 'Substance' compilation album, which I still think covers their best work. I've listened to that album for 22 years and I still haven't worn it out. Within that timeframe, naturally, I checked out JD's other work and other compilations have come out. It was odd to discover that during their career the band had actually only released two albums, on EP and 5 singles for the acclaim they've garnered since Curtis's death. But let's set aside the myths and get to the heart of this.<br /><br />I was initially reluctant to give 'Closer' 3 stars. It almost feels like a betrayal, but I have to be honest. I just don't think this is the best place to start. In fact, I think it's the last place to end and it was the end of the band. From my listening I believe Joy Division really had two sides: 1. a very monotonous, repetitive droning, depressive side (e.g. Auto-Suggestion, Candidate, The Eternal) and 2. an unusual, mysterious, edgy side (e.g. Leaders of Men, Digital, No Love Lost). Both sides seemed to be given equal time from the band and can appeal to vastly different groups. I think of the monotonous side (1.) as the roots of true 'goth' music, and perhaps the least adventurous side of the band. How challenging is it to play the same slow chords for five minutes? But the other side of Joy Division (2.) is what hooked me and what I still think is where they just have no equal. Larger themes are lyrically explored, the beats are quick and steady but unobtrusive, the guitar is quasi-exotic and the bass lines frame it all, carrying the mood with bold punctuation. Some complain Ian's voice is out of tune, and yes, that's completely true. In my opinion that out of tune quality doesn't play well for slow songs as well as the fast, adventurous songs. But mind you, it's just those qualities which make it so rightfor the faster, adventurous side of Joy Division. It's disturbing and on the edge of exploding which is really what I think Joy Division is about. You could throw a pity party listening to 'Closer' whereas you could start a revolutionary terrorist group with 'An Ideal for Living'. Two completely different sides of Joy Division, perhaps for two different types of fans.<br /><br />That said, I'd love to give this album more than 3 stars but my heart is in their earlier work and compilations. If I were new Joy Division listener I would likely start with the 'Warsaw' compilation album. It doesn't get any better than that. After you've worn that out (you won't) then you might step into the moodier side of JD.

I mean, it is not terrible, but it is not great either. It would flow more nicely if she simply got rid of the repetition. For instance, maybe instead of having the main character blush so much, perhaps she could do something else to show discomfort? Doing so will eliminate repetition, but at the same time show readers that the main character is feeling uneasy about something.<br /><br />Now that I got the cons out of the way, let me focus on the pros and why I gave it 4 stars: This book originally worried me when people said there is a lot of sex in it. 085490087X|5.0|[[VIDEOID:mo2GS6FFVP0NR7Y]]<br /><br />OUR SPLENDID LANDSCAPE HAS DEVELOPED LEGALLY<br /><br />An appreciation by Phillip Taylor MBE and Elizabeth Taylor of Richmond Green Chambers<br /><br />As histories go, this splendid and grand story will have a wide range of admirers for the straightforward way in which the beautiful English landscape has developed over the centuries at the direction of the law itself.<br /><br />The focus of the book concerns the role of English Land Law as a pivotal shaper of the modern landscape through the ages. The book's story is told in chronological order with an excellent Foreword by Fiona Reynolds from the National Trust.  We hope to see this book in the Trust's shops in future because it will be of great interest to Trust members visiting its properties.<br /><br />The story starts with pre-history, covering Roman and Anglo-Saxon times.  It then reviews the creation of today's English land law as we know it by the Normans with substantial developments in the Middle Ages as the Conquest changed the face of the country with land being the most important form of property ownership.<br /><br />Christopher Jessel neatly describes the way in which landed property set the agenda for `society' in the 17th century when ownership rights became protected to such an extent that land rights prevented real change until the introduction of the rules against perpetuities and accumulations and many other land reforms.<br /><br />The history of our landscape shows how laws were gradually introduced which adapted or circumvented the original interests in land granted originally by the King. Parliamentary intervention also rearranged the landscape with measures such as the Inclosure Awards and the Prescription Act which are still of relevance today. With the Industrial Revolution came the building of roads, canals and railways which developed many areas from small communities to towns and cities of today.<br /><br />Our landscape today is the envy of the world, especially if one flies over the countryside to see its present beauty as such a special landscape. Jessel concludes this delightful and engaging story with a view of what is happening today: the era of public access to land, laws to protect the environment, and the impact of European legislation which will appeal to the general readership as well as historians and lawyers: the book has pride of place in our bookcase.he world, especially if one flies over the countryside to see its present beauty as such a special landscape. 1451683405|2.0|Sam Harris, to begin with, is to be highly commended for bringing into the spotlight an issue that generationally we have taken for granted;  Do we have free will?  His concept and proofs leading to his ultimate conclusion are well done and, for the most part, highly convincing.  It is an easy read, short and to the point and I strongly advise readers to peruse this essay when they have an opportunity.<br /><br />But, having had a career in Mental Health, leads me to a slightly more skewed conclusion.  It starts with answering the question of \\"How do we explain this process when people actually do make significant personality changes?\\".  At this point the author's treatise fails dramatically.  While Mr. Harris is absolutely correct in identifying the controlling power of the subconscious on the actions that are caused through genetical imprinting or learned psychosocial behavior patterns, he does not take the agent of change into consideration.  To him if a child is genetically born into an physically abusive family, he, too, will automatically become an abuser.  Even though such is the case the majority of the time, all behaviors, like abusiveness, can be overcome.  The steps necessary to do this is, through long term and diligent 'reprogramming', to flood the driving subconscious with conscious reasoning of why you do not want this behavior pattern to exist in your life.  The subconscious is almost like a simple balance scale;  on one side you have '100 pounds' of data and impulses that encourages and drives a person toward further abuse, on the other side you must add conscious reasoning in order to offset these impulses.  Once you have '100 pounds' on the non-abusive side, the thoughts and impressions regarding this action have a 50:50 chance of being either abusive or non-abusive.  Obviously, to get to the point of higher and higher favorability of having a non-abusive thought emerge from a given situation, you are required to add more and more and more rational and peaceful thoughts to the non-abusive thoughts on our imaginary scale. Will the original psychosocial thoughts and impulses to abuse another ever completely go away?  No, but they can become more and more unlikely to appear as the opposite side of the scale is added to.<br /><br />So, in spite of the fact that I applaud Mr. Harris's treatise and the thoughts that went behind it, we are not doomed to future failures based on our uncontrolled pasts.  But, to be frank, the number of persons/clients that I have encountered over time that are able to make the commitment to make these drastic and time consuming changes and to undergo the difficult work that is necessary is highly limited.  Far less than 10% of our population are willing and/or able to make these changes.  The work becomes too hard, the time involved takes too long and the social structure around them discourages dissimilarity within their group.  So, because of this small percentage of consciously active persons, the author, from the sidelines, can obviously view conscious change as not taking place and, therefore, we must operate out of an unchangeable subconscious.  Fortunately, for those who demand more out of their existence other than the morass in which they were raised or the susceptibility of the genes they inherited, there is a clear path towards change.  It may be hard, it may be tedious and may even seem fruitless at times, but it is there.l and peaceful thoughts to the non-abusive thoughts on our imaginary scale. Will the original psychosocial thoughts and impulses to abuse another ever completely go away?  No, but they can become more and more unlikely to appear as the opposite side of the scale is added to.<br /><br />So, in spite of the fact that I applaud Mr. Harris's treatise and the thoughts that went behind it, we are not doomed to future failures based on our uncontrolled pasts.  But, to be frank, the number of persons/clients that I have encountered over time that are able to make the commitment to make these drastic and time consuming changes and to undergo the difficult work that is necessary is highly limited.  Far less than 10% of our population are willing and/or able to make these changes.  The work becomes too hard, the time involved takes too long and the social structure around them discourages dissimilarity within their group.  So, because of this small percentage of consciously active persons, the author, from the sidelines, can obviously view conscious change as not taking place and, therefore, we must operate out of an unchangeable subconscious.  Fortunately, for those who demand more out of their existence other than the morass in which they were raised or the susceptibility of the genes they inherited, there is a clear path towards change.  It may be hard, it may be tedious and may even seem fruitless at times, but it is there.

When it's the RTF core jamming (Chick, Stan, Lenny) with the two new sidemen (JLP and Frank) silent or just comping chords, the trio really COOKS. Nonetheless, I will say that Frank's solo on this tune is quite tasty.<br /><br />JLP shines in duet with Chick on Shadow of Lo, which transitions into Sorceress. The group quickly moves the tune into a hard rock break with Frank soloing over the top. This didn't work for me. Back to the familiar Sorceress riffs with Stanley taking the lead. Ah that's more like it. Slide into Chick's piano solo break and we're off and running. Again, the trio rocks it. Who are these other two guys again?<br /><br />Renaissance<br />I like JLP as a sideman, but his compositions are monotony with a fusion beat. I went through a JLP phase in the 70s and I'm over it. The main benefit to listening to his band was that it turned me on to Allan Holdsworth. (Holdsworth in RTF is an interesting proposition.) This tune? Yawn.<br /><br />Cosmic Rain<br />This is the best tune on the set. The band really brings something new with this effort. Unfortunately you have to get through the first 3 minutes before things really get cranked up. JLP takes a nice turn, evolving into a syncopated Latin motif. Chick picks it up with some lush synth chords that slide into a way cool Fender/Moog shuffle anchored by Lenny, with Stan walking the Alembic. So sweet! Give me two hours of this trio and I'll be happy! Then Stan takes his turn with what can only be described as a Flamenco bass solo.<br /><br />Romantic Warrior<br />Five minutes of noodling before you get to the head. Finally. Things start to move. I'm not crazy about Frank's voicing on the acoustic here. Too much twang. This is an acoustic piece and his axe sounds overly processed. Did I mention that trio? Lenny does some tasty work on this one. Chicks piano solo is classic. These 3 cook with gas.<br /><br />Spain<br />We all know this one. Chick starts it off, joined by JLP. Very nice. Ensemble joins in for the melody. Lenny gets a chance in the spotlight and doesn't let us down. Cool stuff. Chick solos on Fender with crowd sing-along. I could do without these audience participation efforts. I'd rather hear the true musicians work their craft.<br /><br />School Days<br />This tune just feels out of place to me on an RTF effort. School Days is Stan's \\"Free Bird\\" or \\"Stairway to Heaven\\". Do we really need another live version of this anthem? Don't get me wrong, SC is one of my favorite performers. I've seen him live so many times in clubs, duet (with Hiromi), with RTF, and blowing the roof off large arenas (New Barbarians, Atlanta Omni). Every time I've seen him he doesn't disappoint. But can we give School Days a rest?<br /><br />Beyond the 7th Galaxy<br />Sounds surprisingly true to the original studio effort with Stan's fat bass sound. A nice end to the set.<br /><br />Overall some really fine musical moments combined with a few misses, for me anyway. Four stars for the audio portion. Two stars for the DVD. Three stars average.

Valtari sounds like baby bed time music. Which might not be a bad thing for maybe parents who want to expose their babies to good bands right from a very early age. But for me, this just doesn't do it. I've heard this album more than a few times now and I just fail to understand why Sigur Ros would make songs that have minimal to zero impact emotionally and thematically. Every song is easily forgettable. There is not one song on this album that stands out and something that I would be humming much later after having listened to it. Valtari has each song laid out in this sort of a bland theme wherein there are violins and vocal hymns in the backdrop with certain chimes and wind instrument and Jonsi singing a similar sounding melody in his usual monotonous style in EVERY song. Heck I am a musician and I could make this entire album using presets from Omnisphere and be done with it in a week's time.<br /><br />Guys I don't mean to be insulting, but after being such a loyal fan and waiting for so long for a new album by Sigur Ros (not Jonsi solo, not boy meets girl or Alex and whoever....I am talking purely Sigur Ros), when I get something like Valtari, it feels like some left over crumbs just got thrown my way. At this point I do not want to assume that this is the direction SR wants to head in and I truly hope its not the case but unfortunately I just cannot recommend Valtari as a good, fulfilling purchase.<br /><br />In the end here is what I have to say to round up this review: If you are a new listener wanting to enter the world of Sigur Ros I would strongly recommend Agaetis Byrjun, Takk or even Med Sud because if you start with Untitled or Valtari you could develop a warped sense of assessment of SR's music. For me personally their more musically active stuff feels much more satisfying and truly unparalleled. But Valtari and Untitled seem very monotonous and indistinguishable and that qualifies for an album to start getting played in massage parlors and acupuncture clinics. Why does Valtari still get 2 stars and not one? Its because I believe that this band is one of its kind. There is no other that can match SR in their genius of song writing and coming up with themes that are very intense & rich. And even musically within Valtari there is a lot going on purely in terms of sounds. But in the end a song is not just a collection of sounds and layers, it is an overall package. And the overall SONGS are what seriously lack color & soul in Valtari. A minor exception being Flogur Piano. Listen to that when compared to the rest of the album and compare that song to some of their older songs andyou will know exactly what I am talking about.

B007I2BZIE|5.0|OK. First, I am an amateur audiophile. I majored in music and spent two years in college miking and mixing live bands at the soundboard for the college TV station. So my ears hear everything - the great to the terrible - sometimes my brain is independent of my ears, wanting so much to love Rush's Vapor Trails in 2002. I spent a week trying to tweak my new 5.1 surround sound system, thinking I had it set wrong, before I started to see the complaints online regarding the HORRID production on Vapor Trails. Alex said one thing at the time - the band had pushed past digital 0 into distortion during recording - then we heard nothing about it from Rush until last year, when Alex confirmed they were NEVER happy with the production on VT, but, to my sheer delight, that the album IS being remixed and remastered. There will be no re-recording, but they will fix it in mastering where they can. Oh happy day for this Rush fan of 30+ years!<br /><br />Why the above commentary? Because I have read many reviews here about the \\"horrible\\" or \\"awful\\" *insert your favorite adjective* sound quality on Clockwork Angels. While I agree the sound quality is not always stellar, it is nowhere NEAR what VT was! However, everybody's opinion is correct with their reviews of CA, because listening to music is a subjective thing. Many of us are musicians, many of us are Rush fans. And, we are all equal as Rush fans. I was not a fan of Snakes and Arrows. I prefer my Rush to rock - my favorite albums being Permanent Waves, Moving Pictures, and Counterparts (minus Stick It Out - sorry Rush fans). All 3 have been tied for first place, depending on my mood. So after not really enjoying S&A, I approached this new album with a tentative \\"we will see.\\" I liked the first three songs, Caravan, BU2B, and especially Headlong Flight, well enough to download ahead of the album's release. Then I read some reviews, and downloaded the entire album to my iPod Video 5.5, which has great sound quality having the original Wolfson sound chip. I listened through my Koss PortaPro headphones (yes, these are quite awesome :-). I did hear some issues with overlaying of guitars and muddy guitars, especially during the song \\"Clockwork Angels.\\" But I loved ALL these songs SO much. So knowing the CD would sound better (comparing the download of MP3's AAC quality vs Apple Lossless Encoder upload to iPod), I bought that too. I listened first through my Sony CD portable and high quality Sennheiser headphones. Ah-Ha! I could hear much more, but there was still room for improvement, so next I listened through my regular system with B&W speakers, and also through my Harmon Kardon amp with the Sennheisers. Either way, double Ah-Ha! I could hear EVERYTHING that was important to me. Oh major happy delight! What an unbelievable great album Rush has given us with Clockwork Angels. There are no throwaway tracks; I hardly notice that 66 minutes have gone by listening from start to finish. My favorite song at the moment is \\"Seven Cities of Gold\\" with that great rock riff that makes my head bop - reminding me of my other favorite band, Porcupine Tree, and their work from 2002 and 2005. If you've ever seen Steven Wilson rocking out, hair bopping in his face, that's me listening to \\"Seven Cities.\\" Sheer DELIGHT. I love the ballads just as much, however, \\"Halo Effect\\" and the much mentioned \\"The Garden,\\" which may be the best ballad Rush has ever done. I love the pop hooks of \\"The Wreckers.\\" Frankly, the entire album, with the added storyline from Neil, is such a joy to listen to - I've probably listened about 20 times now!<br /><br />My advice is to listen to this album on the best equipment you can. I trashed my iPod MP3 album; it just doesn't compare to the broadness and clarity I hear with the CD and better sound equipment. If you can, get a pair of quality headphones - find out what sounds better to YOU - because that's what is most important. And for everyone it will be different. We Rush fans tend toover-analyze their albums at times, but it's only because we care SO much about the music, and hold it all so dear. I thank you Rush - for your 40 years of brilliant musicianship and great albums. Clockwork Angels has been added to my favorite 3 Rush albums of all time, so now there are 4, with Clockwork Angels edging near the very top.

There's a superbly-staged car chase in Cape Town, a rooftop foot chase in a Johannesburg shanty town, and a number of hand-to-hand fighting sequences that are among the most believable (and, by extension, among the most gruesome) of any I've seen anytime recently in a mainstream Hollywood release ... all of which may at least partly explain why \\"Safe House\\" was such a huge, unqualified hit, not only in the U.S., but in the rest of the world as well (dazzling action set pieces tend to quite easily transcend language barriers).<br /><br />But \\"Safe House\\" is also, at times, surprisingly MOVING as well -- which I think is another reason why this very smart and well-executed drama stayed with me longggg after I first it in a theater this past winter. There's a very palpable and powerful emotional gravity that runs throughout this movie, one that you almost never find in a big-budget Hollywood movie with this many dazzling action sequences in it (Michael Bay could definitely learn A LOT from Espinosa!!)<br /><br />Ultimately, \\"Safe House\\" is a movie about the truly destructive nature of lies -- what they do to those of us who tell them ... and also what they do to those of us who are on the receiving end of them. Government officials around the globe would do well to heed this film's cautionary tale.<br /><br />Sumptuous cinematography by Oliver Wood (\\"Die Hard 2\\", \\"Face/Off\\", the three \\"Bourne\\" movies), with a haunting and spare score by Ramin Djawadi.

And this constantly-surprising remake is proof-positive that you actually CAN have a father character in a film who genuinely DOES care about his daughter and who isn't merely an unceasingly-cartoonish/bufoonish, Bible-thumping caricature of one.<br /><br />\\"Footloose\\" (2011) is  movie with a lot of heart, soul, and wisdom in it. 0765316994|5.0|Andrew Dahl is an ensign in the Universal Union in the 25th century. He has just been assigned as a crew member aboard the Intrepid, a famous flagship of the UU. Obviously thrilled and excited to join the xenobiology laboratory, he is eager to join the ranks and put his knowledge to good use. It starts out well enough with him meeting some other newcomers and becoming friends until things get weird. Away missions are very common on this vessel, but so are deadly encounters with extraterrestrials, the deaths of low ranking officers, and the bizarre survival of the ship's captain, Lieutenant Kerensky, and the chief science officer despite any sort of injury or disease. The older crew members know this and tend to avoid the missions or throw the new crew members into it to save themselves. Why are the mortality rates for this ship so high? Will Andrew even live to figure out why?<br /><br />John Scalzi is one my favorite science fiction writers ever. His Old Man's War series is made of awesome and if you haven't read it, you need to right now. I was dying to know what he would do with the poor, hapless and nameless characters that died practically each episode in Star Trek. I expected fun and silliness (of which there is plenty), but I was pleasantly surprised that Redshirts had so much more depth to it. It's kind of like a mixture Star Trek and Cabin in the Woods. This book is very meta and self aware. It plays with and pokes fun of the conventions of science fiction while managing to be innovative and new at the same time. I don't want to post spoilers, but the plot twist is awesome and very unexpected. It opens up a philosophical discussion of existence and the purpose of life alongside the perpetuation, condemnation, and examination of sci-fi tropes.<br /><br />The characters made the book amazing for me. Unlike their Star Trek counterparts, they are fully fleshed out characters with diverse backgrounds, extensive training, interesting personalities, and hilariouscomedic timing. I liked seeing their world through their perspective. Usually shows are focused on higher ranked characters, but, through Dahl and his friends' eyes, we view the ship from a much different point of view. The codas at the ends are my favorite part because they detail what the ramifications of the resolution of the story are and they pack an emotional punch. One of them even brought me to tears, which was totally unexpected from what I thought was a light summer read.<br /><br />Redshirts is a half parody half homage to Star Trek that works on a variety of levels. It's one of my favorite reads this year. I highly recommend this funny, emotional, and thought provoking book to any fan of sci-fi.

B007I2BZIE|5.0|You know it's a masterpiece when the first three songs blow you away and end up being the weakest tracks on the album. I'd say Caravan, BU2B, and Clockwork Angels continue the sound the band has been exploring the last two decades. These songs could easily fit on albums like Counterparts, Test For Echo, or Vapor Trails; all great albums I might add. The Anarchist completely stopped me in my tracks. This track hearkens back to the sounds of Signals, but with a little harder edge; an instant classic. Following is the track Carnies. The intro is quite unorthodox for Rush; Alex' guitar playing reminds me of John Petrucci. The track quickly morphs into something more \\"Rush-like\\". It could easily fit on Roll The Bones or Counterparts and is another highlight of the album. Alex' brief solo on this track reminds me of his solo on Leave That Thing Alone. Following Carnies is Halo Effect; a nice break from the awesome wall of sound that precedes it. This track is the album's \\"Resist\\". I love when the boys break out the acoustic guitars. This may be the most underrated song on the album; very emotional and beautifully executed. The following three tracks may be the strongest on the album: Seven Cities of Gold; The Wreckers; and, Headlong Flight. The comparison to older albums is growing old so I will just say that these three tracks are vintage Rush. Not vintage in the sense that they sound exactly like previous works; more in the sense that they showcase a band at the height of its artistry and technical proficiency. Geddy's bass playing really shines on these three tracks. Alex' solo on Headlong Flight sounds like his early eighties guitar work. The album finishes on two very strong tracks; Wish Them Well and The Garden. Snakes And Arrows is not one of my favorite Rush albums, but there are still a few tracks I really love from that album. Wish Them Well sounds to me like the best parts of S&A. A lot of people are singing the praises of the album closer The Garden. This track doesn't have such a strong impact on me, but I still think it's solid. \\"Solid\\" is the word I would use to describe Clockwork Angels. It may not have big hits like The Spirit of Radio or Tom Sawyer, but what it does have is consistently great songs throughout the entire album; there is not one weak track. CA is also the first concept album Rush has produced since the 70's. Actually, it may technically be the only concept album Rush has ever produced as those 70's albums always included a handful of tracks that were unrelated to the overarching theme. Speaking of the lyrics, I realize I haven't said a word about Mr. Peart's contribution to CA. Let's face it, Neil is such an outstanding drummer and lyricist that it's often easy to take him for granted. There's no question that Neil is a huge part of what makes Rush...well, Rush! I suppose I've just missed Alex, who I feel has made a big comeback on this album. This is ironic because Alex' disdain for keys made the last few albums more guitar driven, but somehow his distinct sound got lost in all the layering. I feel like his presence is much stronger on CA; sometimes less can be more. :) I would say that Clockwork Angels is right up there with the best albums Rush has ever produced. This is pretty impressive when you consider that this band's first album came out almost 40 years ago. Clockwork Angels combines the greatest elements of the band's last three decades. I personally love each era of this extraordinary band's history so Clockwork Angels is a real treat.
